The Triangle Inequality says that the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the third side. That means $8+8=16$ must be greater than the length of the third side. The third side has a whole number length, so the greatest possible length is 15 units. That makes the perimeter $8+8+15=\boxed{31}$ units.

Sure, let's solve that problem together! 

Let's use the Triangle Inequality Theorem, which states that the sum of any two sides of a triangle must be greater than the third side. If $a$, $b$, and $c$ are the lengths of the sides of a triangle, then:

$$a+b>c$$
$$b+c>a$$
$$a+c>b$$

If we let the third side of the triangle have length $x$, then we have:

$$8+8>x$$
$$16>x$$

So the third side must be less than $16$ units long.

In order for the triangle to have the greatest possible perimeter, we want the third side to be as long as possible, which would be $x=15$. This satisfies the Triangle Inequality Theorem, since:

$$8+8>15$$
$$8+15>8$$
$$15+8>8$$

So the three sides of the triangle would be $8$, $8$, and $15$, and the perimeter would be:

$$8+8+15=31$$

So the greatest possible perimeter for the triangle is $31$ units.

We recognize 7, 24, and 25 as a Pythagorean triple. That means the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 7 \cdot 24 = 84.$ The altitudes are then easy to find: $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{7},$ $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{24},$ and $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{25}.$ Then, our answer is $\frac{168}{7} + \frac{168}{24} + \frac{168}{25} = \boxed{37.72}.$

Sure, I'd be happy to help with that!

To solve this problem, we can use the formula $A = \frac12 bh$, where $A$ is the area of the triangle, $b$ is the length of the base, and $h$ is the corresponding altitude to that base. We will first need to find the area of the triangle before finding the sum of its altitudes.

Using Heron's formula, we can calculate the area of this triangle as follows:

$$\begin{aligned} A &= \sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)} \\ &= \sqrt{\frac{7+24+25}{2} \left(\frac{7+24+25}{2}-7\right) \left(\frac{7+24+25}{2}-24\right) \left(\frac{7+24+25}{2}-25\right)} \\ &= \sqrt{28 \cdot 21 \cdot 4 \cdot 3} \\ &= 84 \end{aligned}$$

Now, we need to find the length of each altitude. Let $h_a$, $h_b$, and $h_c$ be the lengths of the altitudes from the vertices opposite sides $a$, $b$, and $c$, respectively. Then we have:

$$\begin{aligned} h_a &= \frac{2A}{a} = \frac{2\cdot 84}{7} = 24 \\ h_b &= \frac{2A}{b} = \frac{2\cdot 84}{24} = 7 \\ h_c &= \frac{2A}{c} = \frac{2\cdot 84}{25} = 6.72 \\ \end{aligned}$$

Finally, we can find the sum of the lengths of the altitudes as:

$$h_a+h_b+h_c = 24+7+6.72 \approx \boxed{37.72}$$

Therefore, the sum of the lengths of the three altitudes in this triangle is approximately 37.72 units.

Since the measure of angle $BAC$ is 42, the other two equal angles of triangle $ABC$ must be $(180 - 42)/2 = 138/2 = 69$ degrees each. We will introduce the point $F$, which is the center of the circle, and draw segments from each vertex to $F$. Since segment $ED$ is tangent to the circle at point $C$, it must be perpendicular to radius $CF$. Angle $BAC$ is bisected by segment $AF$, so angle $FAC$ is 21 degrees. Angle $FCA$ is also 21 degrees since triangle $AFC$ is isosceles. Thus, the measure of angle $ACD$ is $90 - 21 = \boxed{69\text{ degrees}}$, which is the same as the two base angles of triangle ABC. Sure, let's solve this math problem! 

First, let's label some angles in the diagram:

[asy]
import olympiad; import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8));

draw(Circle((0,0),1));
draw((dir(-30).x -1,dir(-30).y-2)--(dir(-30).x+1,dir(-30).y+2));
pair C = dir(-30), B = dir(-114), A = dir(-114-138), D = (dir(-30).x+1,dir(-30).y+2), E = (dir(-30).x -1,dir(-30).y-2);
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
label("$A$",A,N); label("$B$",B,SW); label("$C$",C,dir(0)); label("$D$",D,dir(0)); label("$E$",E,dir(-90));
label("$x$",C,9*dir(50)); label("$y$",B,dir(-25)); label("$42^\circ$",A,2.5*dir(-60));
[/asy]

We want to find the measure of angle $ACD$, which is labeled as $x$ in the diagram. The fact that $AC=AB$ means that angles $BAC$ and $BCA$ are equal, since they are opposite angles in an isosceles triangle.

Using this information, we can find angle $BCA$ as follows:

\begin{align*}
BCA &= 180^{\circ}-BAC-BAC\\
&= 180^\circ-42^\circ-42^\circ\\
&= 96^\circ
\end{align*}

Now, since $ED$ is tangent to the circle at $C$, we know that angle $ECD$ is a right angle, since a tangent line is perpendicular to the radius of the circle at the point of tangency. 

Because angles $ECA$ and $BCA$ add up to $180^\circ$ (since they are opposite angles in a cyclic quadrilateral), we can find angle $ECA$ as follows:

\begin{align*}
ECA &= 180^\circ-BCA\\
&= 180^\circ-96^\circ\\
&= 84^\circ
\end{align*}

Finally, we can use the fact that angles $ECA$, $ECD$, and $ACD$ add up to $180^\circ$, since they form a straight line. We know that $ECA=84^\circ$ and $ECD=90^\circ$, so we can solve for $ACD$ as follows:

\begin{align*}
ACD &= 180^\circ-ECA-ECD\\
&= 180^\circ-84^\circ-90^\circ\\
&= 6^\circ
\end{align*}

Therefore, the measure of angle $ACD$ is $\boxed{6^\circ}$. Hope this helps!

A circular sheet of paper with radius 6 inches has a circumference of $2\pi r = 2 \pi (6) = 12\pi$ inches. Since this sheet of paper is cut into 3 equal sectors, each sector must have an arc length of $1/3$ that of the original circle, so each sector has an arc length of $4\pi$ inches. Because of the way that a cone is formed, the original radius of the circle now becomes the slant height of the cone and the arc length becomes the perimeter of the cone's base. If we let the radius of the cone base be $R$, then we know that the circumference $4\pi$ must equal $2\pi R$, so $2 \pi R = 4 \pi$. Dividing both sides of this equation by $2\pi$, we get that the radius of the cone base is $2$ inches.

We then notice that the radius of the base, the altitude of the cone, and the slant height of the cone form a right triangle where the slant height is the hypotenuse. Since we know both the radius and the slant height, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find the height $h$ of the cone. We have that $2^2 + h^2 = 6^2$, so $h^2=32$. Taking the square root of both sides, we have that $h=\sqrt{32}=\boxed{4\sqrt{2}}$ inches.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure! Let me help you with that.

The first thing we need to find is the radius of the base of each cone. Since the circular sheet of paper has a radius of 6 inches and is divided into 3 equal sectors, each sector will have an arc length of 2π/3 (since the circumference of a circle is 2π times its radius, and we are dividing the circle into 3 equal sectors). Therefore, the radius of each base will be the length of the arc of each sector, which is:

r = (2π/3)/2π x 6 = 2 inches

Next, we need to find the slant height of each cone. Since the cone is formed by taking a sector and joining its two straight edges together, the slant height will be the radius of the circular sector. Therefore, the slant height of each cone will also be 2 inches.

Finally, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find the height of each cone. The height (h) is the remaining leg of a right triangle, with the slant height (s) as one leg and the radius (r) as the other leg. Therefore, we have:

h^2 = s^2 - r^2 = 2^2 - 2^2 = 0

Since the height squared equals 0, the height must be 0 as well. Therefore, the height of each cone is 0 inches.

I hope that helps you! Let me know if you have any other questions.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure! Let us first note that the formula for the volume of a cylinder is $V = \pi r^2h$, where $r$ is the radius of the base and $h$ is the height.

In this problem, the volume is given as $2700\pi$ cubic meters, and the radius of the base is $30$ meters. Substituting these values into the formula, we get:

$$
2700\pi = \pi (30^2)h
$$

Simplifying this equation, we get:

$$
2700 = 900h
$$

Dividing both sides by $900$, we get:

$$
h = 3
$$

Therefore, the height of the water tank is $3$ meters.

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $BC$, $BE=EC$. Since triangles $\triangle ABE$ and $\triangle AEC$ have equal base length and share the same height, they have the same area.

$\triangle ABC$ has $\frac{1}{2}$ the area of the rectangle, so the white triangle, $\triangle AEC$, has $1/4$ the area of the rectangle.

Hence the shaded region has $1 - \frac{1}{4}=\frac{3}{4}$ of the area of the rectangle, or $\boxed{75} \%$.

Sure! 

Firstly, let's call the rectangle's width $w$. Since the rectangle is four times as long as it is wide, the length of the rectangle is $4w$. 

Secondly, since we know point $E$ is the midpoint of $\overline{BC}$, we can say that the width of the rectangle's lower triangle is also $w$. 

Thirdly, we can use trigonometry to find the length of $\overline{AE}$. We know that the angle $\angle BAE$ is $\text{tan}^{-1}(1/4)$ (i.e. the arctangent of $1/4$). We can use the tangent function to find that the length of $\overline{AE}$ is $\frac{w}{2\text{tan}(\text{tan}^{-1}(1/4))}=\frac{w}{2/5}=\frac{5}{2}w$. 

Now we can find the area of the rectangle by using the formula $A=lw$, where $l$ is the length and $w$ is the width. The area of the rectangle is $4w^2$. 

The area of the shaded region consists of the area of the rectangle's lower triangle and the area of the rectangle's upper triangle. The area of each triangle is $\frac{1}{2}(\text{base})(\text{height})$. 

The height of each triangle is $w$, and the base of each triangle is $\frac{5}{2}w$. Therefore, the area of each triangle is $\frac{1}{2}(w)(\frac{5}{2}w)=\frac{5}{4}w^2$. 

So the total area of the shaded region is $2(\frac{5}{4}w^2)=\frac{5}{2}w^2$. 

Finally, we can find what percent of the rectangle is shaded by dividing the area of the shaded region by the area of the rectangle and multiplying by $100\%$. 

$\frac{\frac{5}{2}w^2}{4w^2}=\frac{5}{8}$, so the shaded region makes up $\frac{5}{8}$ of the rectangle. Multiplying by $100\%$, we get that the shaded region makes up $\boxed{62.5\%}$ of the rectangle.

For $\triangle ABC$ to be acute, all angles must be acute.

For $\angle A$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie above the line passing through $A$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $P(4,0)$ and $Q(0, 4)$.

For $\angle B$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie below the line through $B$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $S(14,0)$ and $T(0, 14)$.

For $\angle C$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie outside the circle $U$ that has $\overline{AB}$ as a diameter.

Let $O$ denote the origin. Region $R$, shaded below, has area equal to \begin{align*}
\text{Area}(\triangle OST) - \text{Area}(\triangle OPQ) - \text{Area(Circle }U) &= \frac{1}{2}\cdot 14^2 - \frac{1}{2}\cdot 4^2 -
\pi\left(\frac{\sqrt{50}}{2}\right)^{\hspace{-3pt}2}\\
&= \boxed{90 - \frac{25}{2}\pi}.
\end{align*}[asy]
pair T,Q,O,P,J,A,B;
P=(3,0);
J=(11.4,0);

[/asy]

Of course! This is a geometry problem, so let's start by plotting the points $A$ and $B$ on a coordinate plane:

[asy]
size(6cm);
pair A=(2,2),B=(7,7);
fill((11,0)--(0,0)--(0,11)--cycle, lightcyan);
draw((0,0)--(11,0),EndArrow);
draw((0,0)--(0,11),EndArrow);
label("$x$",(11,0),E);
label("$y$",(0,11),N);
draw(A--B--(2,7)--cycle);
label("$A$",A,SW);
label("$B$",B,NE);
pair C=foot(B,A,(2,7));
draw(rightanglemark(B,C,A,10));
filldraw( (2,2)--(2,7)--(7,7)--cycle, heather+white+white,1.0bp);
label("$C$",C,S);
[/asy]

We know we want all points $C$ that make $\triangle ABC$ an acute triangle, which means that the smallest angle of $\triangle ABC$ needs to be $\lt 90^\circ$. By definition, the smallest angle of a triangle is opposite the shortest side, so we want to find all points $C$ such that $AC^2+BC^2>AB^2$. Let $C=(x,y)$ be any point in the first quadrant. Then, we have

\begin{align*}
AC^2+BC^2 &= (x-2)^2+(y-2)^2+(x-7)^2+(y-7)^2 \\
&=2x^2+2y^2-18x-18y+98 \\
&= 2(x^2-9x+y^2-9y) + 80.
\end{align*}

We want $AC^2+BC^2>AB^2$, or

$$2(x^2-9x+y^2-9y)+80 > 5^2+5^2=50,$$

which simplifies to

$$x^2-9x+y^2-9y>5.$$ 

To graph this inequality, we can rearrange it to get

$$(x-4.5)^2+(y-4.5)^2>30.25.$$ 

This is the inequality for the region outside the circle centered at $(4.5,4.5)$ with radius $\sqrt{30.25}=5.5$. Since we want the region only in the first quadrant, we only need to consider the portion of the circle in the first quadrant.

[asy]
size(6cm);
filldraw(circle((4.5,4.5),5.5), lightyellow,1.0bp);
draw((0,0)--(11,0),EndArrow);
draw((0,0)--(0,11),EndArrow);
label("$x$",(11,0),E);
label("$y$",(0,11),N);
label("$4.5$",(4.5,0),S);
label("$4.5$",(0,4.5),W);
filldraw( (2,2)--(2,7)--(7,7)--cycle, heather+white+white,1.0bp);
label("$A$",(2,2),SW);
label("$B$",(7,7),NE);
fill(circle((4.5,4.5),5.5), white);
pair C1=(5.5,0), C2=(-0.02,5.2);
draw((0,0)--C1,EndArrow);
draw((0,0)--C2,EndArrow);
draw(C2--C1);
label("$5.5$",C1,S);
label("$5.5$",C2,W);
[/asy]

The area of the circle is $\pi r^2 = 30.25\pi$, and the area of the quadrant is $(1/4)(11)(11)=30.25$. Therefore, the area of the region $R$ is just the area of the quadrant minus the area of the circular sector, or

$$(1/4)(11)(11) - (1/4)(5.5)^2\pi = \boxed{\frac{121}{4}-\frac{121}{8}\pi}.$$

GPT File: datasets/persona_specific_student/persona_specific_student/geometry/15_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
To find the area of triangle ABC, we can use the formula:

$A = \frac{1}{2}bh,$

where $b$ is the length of the base and $h$ is the height of the triangle.

To find the base $b$, we can subtract the x-coordinates of points $B$ and $C$:

$b = 12 - 0 = 12.$

To find the height $h$, we can draw a perpendicular line from point $A$ to the base. This line intersects the base at a point we'll call $D.$ Notice that we now have two right triangles: $\triangle ABD$ and $\triangle ACD.$

From the Pythagorean theorem, we can find the lengths of the two legs of $\triangle ABD$:

$AB^2 = AD^2 + BD^2,$

$4^2 = AD^2 + 9^2,$

$AD^2 = 16 - 81 = -65.$

We have a problem! This tells us that $\triangle ABD$ is not a right triangle. What about $\triangle ACD$?

$AC^2 = AD^2 + CD^2,$

$12^2 = AD^2 + 9^2,$

$AD^2 = 144 - 81 = 63.$

So, the height of the triangle is $h = AD = \sqrt{63}.$

Applying the formula, we get:

$A = \frac{1}{2}bh,$

$A = \frac{1}{2}(12)(\sqrt{63}),$

$A = 18\sqrt{7}.$

Therefore, the area of $\triangle ABC$ is $18\sqrt{7}.$

We "complete" the truncated cone by adding a smaller, similar cone atop the cut, forming a large cone.  We don't know the height of the small cone, so call it $x$.  Since the small and large cone are similar, we have $x/4=(x+6)/8$; solving yields $x=6$.  Hence the small cone has radius 4, height 6, and volume $(1/3)\pi(4^2)(6)=32\pi$ and the large cone has radius 8, height 12, and volume $(1/3)\pi(8^2)(12)=256\pi$.  The solid's volume is the difference of these two volumes, or $256\pi-32\pi=224\pi$ cubic cm.  Thus we see $n=\boxed{224}$.

To find the volume of the truncated cone, we need to know the volume of the large cone and the small cone.

The volume of a cone is given by the formula $V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2h$, where $r$ is the radius of the base and $h$ is the height.

The volume of the large cone is:

$$V_1 = \frac{1}{3}\pi(8)^2(6) = \frac{256}{3}\pi$$

The volume of the small cone is:

$$V_2 = \frac{1}{3}\pi(4)^2(6) = 32\pi$$

Therefore, the volume of the truncated cone is:

$$V = V_1 - V_2 = \frac{256}{3}\pi - 32\pi = \frac{128}{3}\pi$$

So $n=\boxed{42}$

Yes, I can definitely help you with that. 

A hexagonal prism has 2 hexagonal bases, and 6 rectangular faces. Each rectangular face has two edges adjacent to hexagonal face, and two parallel edges opposite to another rectangular face. The hexagonal bases have 6 edges each. 

Therefore, the total number of edges in a hexagonal prism can be calculated as:
Number of edges in rectangular faces = (6 faces)*(4 edges per face) = 24 edges
Number of edges in the two hexagonal bases = (2 bases)*(6 edges per base) = 12 edges

Total number of edges in a hexagonal prism = Number of edges in rectangular faces + Number of edges in the two hexagonal bases
= 24 edges + 12 edges
= 36 edges

So, a hexagonal prism has 36 edges.

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $AC$, the area of triangle $BCE$ is half the area of triangle $ABC$, or $144/2 = 72$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Since $GN$ is parallel to $BC$, triangles $ENG$ and $ECB$ are similar.  Furthermore, $G$ is the centroid of triangle $ABC$, so the ratio of similarity is $EG/EB = 1/3$.  Therefore, the area of triangle $ENG$ is $72 \cdot (1/3)^2 = \boxed{8}$.

We note that $AD$ is a median of triangle $ABC$, so it divides $BC$ into two equal parts.  Let $BC = 2a$, so $BD = DC = a$.  Similarly, we have $CE = EA = a$ and $AF = FB = a$.  Therefore, $DG = \frac23 AD = \frac23 a$, $EG = \frac23 BE = \frac23 a$, and $FG = \frac23 CF = \frac23 a$.

Since $GM$ is parallel to $BC$, we have $\triangle GMB \sim \triangle ABC$, so $BM = \frac{2}{3}a$.  Similarly, $CN = \frac{2}{3}a$.  Now, let $K$ be the midpoint of $BC$.  Then, by the midpoint formula, $KM = \frac{1}{3}a$ and $KN = \frac{2}{3}a$.

Consider the triangles $KNM$ and $KBC$.  We know that $BC = 2a$, so the area of $\triangle KBC$ is $\frac{1}{2}\cdot 2a \cdot \frac{2}{3}a = \frac{2}{3}a^2$.  Also, $KM = \frac{1}{3}a$ and $KN = \frac{2}{3}a$, so the height of $\triangle KMN$ with respect to $MN$ is $\frac{1}{3}a$ and the height with respect to $BC$ is $\frac{2}{3}a$.  Therefore, the area of $\triangle KMN$ is $\frac{1}{2}bh = \frac{1}{2}\cdot \frac{2}{3}a \cdot \frac{1}{3}a = \frac{1}{9}a^2$.

Finally, we note that $\triangle ENG$ is similar to $\triangle KMN$ with ratio $\frac{3}{2}$, since $EG = \frac23 KM$ and $EN = \frac23 KN$.  Therefore, the area of $\triangle ENG$ is $\left(\frac{3}{2}\right)^2 = \boxed{\frac{9}{4}}\cdot \frac{1}{9}a^2 = \frac{1}{4}a^2$.

I hope that helps! Let me know if you have any questions.

Extracting Answer from: 
The problem asks us to determine the volume of the box that is not occupied by the sphere. We know that the diameter of the sphere is equal to the height of the box, so the diameter is $30$ cm. The radius of the sphere is then $15$ cm. The side of the cube is also $30$ cm.

Let's consider the sphere first. Its volume is $\frac{4}{3}\pi r^3=\frac{4}{3}\pi(15)^3=9,000\pi$ cubic centimeters.

Now, let's consider the cube. Its volume is $30^3=27,000$ cubic centimeters.

The sphere is symmetric within the box, so the amount of the box that the sphere doesn't occupy is the same no matter which side we consider. We can then consider just one quarter of the box, which has volume $\frac{1}{8}(30^3)=3,375$ cubic centimeters. Within this quarter box, there is a sphere with volume $1/8$ of the total sphere volume, or $1,125\pi$ cubic centimeters. The remaining volume of this quarter box is the volume we seek.

Hence, the remaining volume is $$\frac{1}{8}(30^3)-1,125\pi=3,375-1,125\pi=\boxed{3,375(1-\pi)}$$cubic centimeters.

If the point $(a,b)$ is reflected over the $y$-axis, it will land on the point $(-a,b)$.  Thus, $j=-a$ and $k=b$.  We were given that $a+j=0$, and $a+(-a)=0$ so this is satisfied.  From $b+k=0$, we find  $$b+(b)=0\Rightarrow 2b=0$$ $$\boxed{b=0}$$

Sure, let's solve this problem together! 

When a point $(x, y)$ is reflected over the $y$-axis, its new coordinates become $(-x, y)$. 

So, in this case, the point $(a,b)$ reflected over the $y$-axis becomes $(-a,b)$. We know that the new point is $(j,k)$, so we can set up two equations using the given information:

$a+j=0$

$b+k=0$

Solving for $j$ in the first equation gives us $j=-a$. Substituting this into the second equation, we get:

$b+k=0 \Rightarrow b+(-a)=0 \Rightarrow b-a=0 \Rightarrow b=a$

Therefore, we know that $b=a$. But we also know that $a+j=0$, which means $a=-j$. Substituting $a=-j$ into $b=a$ gives us $b=-j$. 

So, the value of $b$ is $\boxed{-j}$.

Sure, this problem can be solved using the triangle inequality theorem, which states that the sum of any two sides of a triangle must be greater than the third side. So in this case, we have:

1 + 3 > x (where x is the length of the third side)

Simplifying this inequality, we get:

4 > x

Therefore, x must be less than 4 units. But we also know that x must be greater than the difference between the two given sides, which is:

3 - 1 = 2

So, combining these two inequalities, we have:

2 < x < 4

The only integer value of x that satisfies this inequality is 3 units, so the length of the third side is 3 units.

$\triangle DBC$ has base $\overline{BC}$ of length 8 and height $\overline{BD}$ of length 3; therefore, its area is $\frac{1}{2}\times8\times 3=12$.

The area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ equals the area of $\triangle DBC$ minus the area of $\triangle FEC$.

$\triangle FEC$ has base $EC=BC-BE=8-4=4$. The height of $\triangle FEC$ is equal to the vertical distance from point $F$ to the $x$-axis, which is equal to the $y$-coordinate of point $F$, or 2. Therefore, the area of $\triangle FEC$ is $\frac{1}{2}\times4\times 2=4$.

Finally, the area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ is $12-4=\boxed{8}$.

GPT File: datasets/persona_specific_student/persona_specific_student/geometry/331_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's solve this math problem! To find the area of $\triangle QPS$, we need to find the length of $QS$ first.

Since $\triangle PRQ$ is an isosceles triangle with $QR = 8$ and $\angle PRQ = 120^\circ$, we can use the Law of Cosines to find $PQ$:
\begin{align*}
PQ^2 &= PR^2 + QR^2 - 2 \cdot PR \cdot QR \cdot \cos \angle PRQ \\
&= 12^2 + 8^2 - 2 \cdot 12 \cdot 8 \cdot \cos 120^\circ \\
&= 208.
\end{align*}
Therefore, $PQ = \sqrt{208}.$

Now, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find $QS$:
\begin{align*}
QS &= \sqrt{RS^2 - RQ^2} \\
&= \sqrt{(RS+RP)^2 - RQ^2} \\
&= \sqrt{(RS^2 + RP^2 + 2 \cdot RS \cdot RP) - RQ^2} \\
&= \sqrt{(RS^2 + RP^2 + 2 \cdot RS \cdot RP - QR^2) + QR^2 - RQ^2} \\
&= \sqrt{(RS + RP)^2 - PQ^2} \\
&= \sqrt{(QS)^2 - 208}.
\end{align*}
Simplifying the last expression gives $QS^2 - (QS)^2 + 208$, or $208$. Therefore, $QS = \sqrt{208}$.

Finally, we can find the area of $\triangle QPS$ using the formula for the area of a triangle:

$$\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \cdot QS \cdot PS \cdot \sin \angle QPS.$$

Since $\angle RPS = 90^\circ$ and $\angle PRQ = 120^\circ$, we have $\angle QPS = 30^\circ$ (because the angles in a triangle add up to $180^\circ$), and $PS = PR - RS = 12 - \sqrt{208}$.

Plugging in the values, we get:

$$\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \sqrt{208} \cdot (12 - \sqrt{208}) \cdot \sin 30^\circ \approx \boxed{32.0}.$$

Since $\angle WAX = 90^\circ$ regardless of the position of square $ABCD$, then $A$ always lies on the semi-circle with diameter $WX$.

The center of this semi-circle is the midpoint, $M$, of $WX$.

To get from $P$ to $M$, we must go up 4 units and to the left 3 units (since $WX=2$), so $PM^2=3^2+4^2=25$ or $PM=5$.

Since the semi-circle with diameter $WX$ has diameter 2, it has radius 1, so $AM=1$.

So we have $AM=1$ and $MP=5$.

Extracting Answer from: 
To find the maximum possible distance from $A$ to $P$, we need to find the point $P$ that is farthest from $A$ among all the possible options.

We first notice that $AP \leq AB+BP$, by the triangle inequality. Let $E$ be the midpoint of $BC$, and $F$ be the midpoint of $CD$. We then have $BE=EC=CF=FD=3$, and $AF=2\sqrt{2}$. Additionally, $\angle BAF = \angle ABE = 45^\circ$, so $\triangle ABE$ is an isosceles right triangle, and $\angle BAE = \angle AEB = 22.5^\circ$. Similarly, $\triangle APF$ is an isosceles right triangle, and $\angle PAF = \angle AFP = 45^\circ$. As a result, $\angle BAP = \angle BAE + \angle EAF + \angle PAF = 112.5^\circ$.

Assuming that $P$ is in the interior of $ABCD$, we then use the following construction: draw the line passing through $A$ and parallel to $BC$, and draw the line passing through $A$ and parallel to $CD$. Let $G$ be where these two lines intersect (the intersection cannot be on the line that contains $AD$, $P$, and $G$, since that would mean $AP$ is parallel to $BC$ or $CD$, which is impossible).

[asy]
unitsize(0.4 cm);

pair a, b, c, d, w, x, y, z, g, h;

a = (5*sqrt(2),5*sqrt(2));
b = (5*sqrt(2),-5*sqrt(2));
c = (-5*sqrt(2),-5*sqrt(2));
d = (-5*sqrt(2),5*sqrt(2));
w = (2*sqrt(2),2*sqrt(2));
x = (8 - 2*sqrt(2),2*sqrt(2));
y = (8 - 2*sqrt(2),8 - 2*sqrt(2));
z = (2*sqrt(2),8 - 2*sqrt(2));
g = (a + reflect(b,c)*(a))/2;
h = (a + reflect(c,d)*(a))/2;

draw(w--x--y--z--cycle);
draw(a--b--c--d--cycle);
draw(a--g--h--cycle);
draw(a--foot(a,b,c));
draw(a--foot(a,c,d));
draw(a--foot(a,w,z),dashed);
draw(rightanglemark(b,g,a,16));
draw(rightanglemark(h,d,a,16));
draw(rightanglemark(w,foot(w,a,b),a,16));
draw(rightanglemark(z,foot(z,a,d),a,16));
draw(w--foot(w,a,b),dashed);
draw(z--foot(z,a,d),dashed);

label("$A$", a, NE);
label("$B$", b, SE);
label("$C$", c, SW);
label("$D$", d, NW);
label("$W$", w, NW);
label("$X$", x, NE);
label("$Y$", y, SE);
label("$Z$", z, SW);
label("$G$", g, S);
label("$H$", h, W);
label("$Q$", foot(a,b,c), S);
label("$R$", foot(a,c,d), W);
[/asy]

We then draw perpendiculars from $A$ to $BC$ and $CD$, intersecting them at $Q$ and $R$ respectively. By basic geometry, we can then show that:
\begin{align*}
GQ&=5\sqrt{2}-2(2+\sqrt{2})=3\sqrt{2}-4, \\
HR&=5\sqrt{2}-2(2+\sqrt{2})=3\sqrt{2}-4.
\end{align*}
Using Pythagoras’ Theorem, we then find that $AG=\sqrt{(2+\sqrt{2})^2+(3\sqrt{2}-4)^2}=3$, and that $AP=AG+GP$. Thus, we need to maximize $GP$.

Looking at $\triangle GBC$, we see that $\angle GBC = 180^\circ - \angle BAF = 135^\circ$. Thus, $\angle GAB = 45^\circ - \angle GBC/2 = 22.5^\circ$. Similarly, $\angle GBA = \angle GAB$, so $\triangle GAB$ is an isosceles right triangle. Thus, $GB=AB/\sqrt{2} = 2\sqrt{2}$.

We then look at $\triangle GPB$. By construction, $\angle GPB = 90^\circ - \angle GBC = 45^\circ$, and so $\triangle GPB$ is an isosceles right triangle. Thus, $GP=GB/\sqrt{2} = 2$. So $AP=3+2=5$.

However, this is not the only possible configuration. In general, we cannot assume that the maximum point $P$ is in the interior of the square $ABCD$, but it is possible that $P$ lies on one of the sides of the square. Since we have shown that $AP\le 5$ for all points inside the square $ABCD$, we only need to check the four sides.

If $P$ lies on $AB$, then $AP=AB=2\sqrt{2}$, which is smaller than 5. Similarly, if $P$ lies on $BC$, then $AP=BP=2$, which is smaller than 5. If $P$ lies on $CD$, then $AP=CD=6$, which is also smaller than 5. Finally, if $P$ lies on $DA$, then $AP$ equals the length of the diagonal of one of the $2\times 2$ squares. By the Pythagorean Theorem, this length is $\sqrt{2^2+2^2}=2\sqrt{2}$, which is smaller than 5.

Therefore, the maximum possible distance from $A$ to $P$ is $\boxed{5}$.

The triangle in the diagram is a $30-60-90$ triangle and has long leg $2\sqrt3$.  Therefore the short leg has length 2 and hypotenuse has length 4.  The bases of the trapezoid are then 5 and $2+5+2=9$ and the legs are both length 4.  The entire trapezoid has perimeter $9+4+5+4=\boxed{22}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! An isosceles trapezoid is a trapezoid whose nonparallel sides are congruent. Let the trapezoid have the following labeling:
[asy]
size(200);
defaultpen(linewidth(0.7));
draw(origin--(9,0)--(7,2*sqrt(3))--(2,2*sqrt(3))--cycle);
label("A", origin, S);
label("B", (9,0), S);
label("C", (7,2*sqrt(3)), N);
label("D", (2,2*sqrt(3)), N);
draw(Label("$a$", align=NE), (7,2*sqrt(3))--(2,2*sqrt(3)));
draw((7,2*sqrt(3))--(6,2*sqrt(3)+3),dashed);
draw((2,2*sqrt(3))--(3,2*sqrt(3)+3),dashed);
label("$60^{\circ}$", (9,0), 3NW,fontsize(9));
label("$60^{\circ}$", origin, 3SW,fontsize(9));
[/asy]
By symmetry, we know that $\angle DCB=60^\circ$ as well. We can also see that $\triangle ADC$ is a 30-60-90 triangle because $\angle ACD=90^\circ$, so $AD=\sqrt{3}\cdot CD$. Let's call the length of the longer base $b$ and the height of the trapezoid $h$. Because the trapezoid is isosceles, we know that the legs of the right triangles formed by dropping an altitude from $B$ and $C$ have the same lengths. Specifically, $AD = h$ (height of trapezoid), and $BC = \frac{b-5}{2}$.

Using our height, we know that:
\[h = AD = \sqrt{3}\cdot CD = \sqrt{3}\cdot (BC+5)\]
\[h = \sqrt{3}\cdot BC + 5\sqrt{3}\]

Similarly, using the Pythagorean theorem on $\triangle BCD$, we have $BC^2+h^2 = (\frac{b-5}{2})^2$. Substituting our expression for $h$ into this equation and solving for $b$, we find that:
\[b^2 - 10b + 155 = 0\]
This quadratic factors as $(b-5)^2 + 30 = 40$, so $b=5+\sqrt{10}$. Using the expression for $BC$, we get $BC=\frac{\sqrt{10}}{2}$ inches.

Finally, the perimeter of the trapezoid is just the sum of its sides:
\[\text{Perimeter} = AB + BC + CD + DA = 5 + \frac{\sqrt{10}}{2} + 5 + h\]
\[= 10 + \sqrt{3}\cdot BC + 5\sqrt{3}\]
\[= 10+\sqrt{3}\cdot \frac{\sqrt{10}}{2} + 5\sqrt{3}\]
\[= 10+\frac{3\sqrt{10}}{2} + 5\sqrt{3}\]
\[= \frac{3\sqrt{10}}{2} + 5\sqrt{3} + 10\approx 23.7\text{ in}\] 

Therefore, the perimeter of the trapezoid is approximately 23.7 inches.

To have a line of symmetry along the diagonal $\overline{BD}$, we must have the same amount of black squares to the left of $\overline{BD}$ as we do to the right of $\overline{BD}$. Since $\overline{BD}$ contains 2 small squares, we want to color half of the total 16 squares black. Therefore, the minimum number of small squares we must color black is $\frac{1}{2}(16) = 8$. ~ Mathandski

The surface area of a sphere with radius $r$ is $4\pi r^2$.  Thus, Theo's sphere has surface area $4\pi(5^2)=100\pi$ and each of Akshaj's spheres have surface area $4\pi(2^2)=16\pi$, so Akshaj's spheres together have surface area $16\pi\cdot 2 = 32\pi$.  The desired ratio is hence $\frac{100\pi}{32\pi} = \boxed{\frac{25}{8}}$.

Sure, let's work on this math problem: 

First, we need to calculate the surface area of each sphere. 

The formula for the surface area of a sphere is: 

SA = 4πr^2

Where SA is the surface area and r is the radius of the sphere. 

So, for Theo's sphere with radius 5: 
SA = 4π(5)^2 = 100π

For Akshaj's two spheres with radius 2: 
SA = 4π(2)^2 = 16π (per sphere)
Total SA of two spheres = 2 × 16π = 32π

Now, let's find the ratio of the surface area of Theo's sphere to the total surface area of Akshaj's spheres: 

Ratio = (Theo's sphere SA) / (Akshaj's spheres SA)
Ratio = 100π / 32π
Ratio = 25/8

Therefore, the ratio of the surface area of Theo's sphere to the total surface area of Akshaj's spheres is 25/8.

DE= \sqrt{3^{2}-\left(\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{3}\right)^{2}}= \frac{\sqrt{69}}{3}.
\]Because $D$ is 1 unit above the plane and the top of the larger sphere is 2 units above $E$, the distance from the plane to the top of the larger sphere is \[

Sure, let's solve the problem you've presented. 

First, let's draw a diagram to help visualize the problem. We will have three small spheres, each with a radius of 1, resting tangent to a horizontal plane. 

Next, we will place a larger sphere with a radius of 2 on top of the small spheres. The goal is to find the distance from the horizontal plane to the top of the larger sphere. 

Now, we can use the Pythagorean theorem to solve for this distance. The height of the stack of three small spheres will be 3 (since each small sphere has a radius of 1). 

Adding this height to the radius of the larger sphere, we get a total height of 5. 

Therefore, the distance from the horizontal plane to the top of the larger sphere is 5. 

So, the answer to the problem is 5 units.

Since $AE = 2$, $EB = 8$, but since $EFGH$ is a square, $EH = EF$, and $AH = EB$ by ASA congruence of right triangles $AHE$ and $BEF$. By the Pythagorean theorem, $(EH)^2 = (AE)^2 + (AH)^2 = 2^2 + 8^2 = \boxed{68}$, which is also the area of square $EFGH$ as the square of one of its sides.

We note that $ABCD$ must have side length 10 since $10^2=100$.  Since $AE=2$, we have $HE=AE=2$ as well.  By vertical angles, $\angle HEG \cong \angle AEB$.  Since $\angle AEB$ is a right angle, it follows that $\angle HEG$ is also a right angle, and so $HEG$ is a right triangle.  Therefore, $EG=5$ and $FG=HE=2$, so $EFGH$, is a square with side length 5 and area $\boxed{25}$ square centimeters.

Sure! Let's start by drawing out the given situation:

[asy]
pair A,B,C,D,H,O,P;
O = (0,0);
P = (6,0);
H = (-6,0);
A = intersectionpoint(arc((A + H)/2, abs(A -  H)/2, 0, 180), arc(O, 2, 0, 180));
D = intersectionpoint(arc((A + P)/2, abs(A -  P)/2, 0, 180), arc(P, 4, 0, 180));
B = reflect(O,P)*(A);
C = reflect(O,P)*(D);
draw(O--P,linewidth(0.7));
draw(Circle(O,2),linewidth(0.7));
draw(Circle(P,4),linewidth(0.7));
draw(interp(A,D,-0.2)--interp(A,D,1.2),linewidth(0.7));
draw(interp(B,C,-0.2)--interp(B,C,1.2),linewidth(0.7));
draw(A--O--B,linewidth(0.7));
draw(D--P--C,linewidth(0.7));
label("$O$",O,SE);
label("$P$",P,E);
label("$D$",D,NW);
label("$A$",A,NW);
label("$B$",B,S);
label("$C$",C,S);
label("2",(-0.45,0.95),E);
label("6",(3,0),N);
label("4",(5.55,1.95),E);

[/asy]

We know that $OA=OB=2$, $PC=PD=4$, and that $AD$ and $BC$ are both tangent to both circles. The first thing we can do is notice that because $AD$ is tangent to both circles, $\angle ADO$ is a right angle (where $O$ is the center of the circle with radius 2). Similarly, $\angle BCO$ is a right angle (where $O$ is the center of the circle with radius 2). 

We can also notice that $\angle ODC=\angle OAD$ (both are angles that are external to the circle with radius 2 and that are formed by lines that are tangent to the circle). Similarly, $\angle OCB=\angle OBA$. 

From these angles, we can see that triangles $OAD$ and $OBC$ are similar (because they both have a right angle and share another angle). So we have \[\frac{OA}{OB}=\frac{OD}{OC}.\] Plugging in the values we know, we get \[\frac{2}{2+4}=\frac{OD}{OC}.\] Solving for $OD/OC$, we get $1/3$.

Now we can use this information to find the area of trapezoid $ABCD$ (which is equivalent to the area of the concave hexagon we want to find). We know that the height of the trapezoid is just $AD=BC$, so we just need to find the length of the top and bottom bases. To do this, we can use the fact that the diagonals of a trapezoid divide each other proportionally.

Let $E$ be the intersection of $AD$ and $BC$. Then $ADE$ and $BCE$ are similar triangles. Since $OE$ is perpendicular to $AD$ (because $\angle ADO$ is a right angle), we know that $\triangle OAE$ is also similar to $ADE$. Similarly, $\triangle OBE$ is similar to $BCE$. So we can find $AE$ and $BE$ as follows:

\begin{align*}
\frac{AE}{DE}&=\frac{OA}{OD},\\
\frac{BE}{CE}&=\frac{OB}{OC}.
\end{align*}

Plugging in the values we know, we get

\begin{align*}
\frac{AE}{2}&=\frac{1}{\frac{2}{3}}=\frac{3}{2},\\
\frac{BE}{4}&=\frac{1}{\frac{1}{3}}=3.
\end{align*}

Therefore, $AE=3$ and $BE=12$. The area of the trapezoid is then \[\frac{(3+12)(AD)}{2}=7.5(4+2)=45.\]

So the area of the concave hexagon $AOBCPD$ is $\boxed{45}$.

Since $PQ=QR$, we have $\angle QPR=\angle QRP$.

Since $\angle PQR + \angle QPR + \angle QRP = 180^\circ$, we have $40^\circ + 2(\angle QRP) = 180^\circ$, so $2(\angle QRP) = 140^\circ$ or $\angle QRP = 70^\circ$.

Since $\angle PRQ$ and $\angle SRT$ are vertical angles, we have $\angle SRT = \angle PRQ = 70^\circ$.

Since $RS=RT$, we have $\angle RST = \angle RTS = x^\circ$.

From $\triangle RST$, we have  $\angle SRT + \angle RST + \angle RTS = 180^\circ$, so $70^\circ + 2x^\circ = 180^\circ$ or $2x = 110$ or $x=\boxed{55}$.

In triangle $QPR$, the three angles are as follows: $\angle QPR = 180 - x - 40$, $\angle QRP = x$, and $\angle QP = 40^\circ$.

Similarly, in triangle $SRT$, the three angles are as follows: $\angle SRT = 180 - x - 40$, $\angle STR = x$, and $\angle ST = 40^\circ$.

simplifying gives:

Sure, let me help you with that!

To solve this problem, we can use the formula for the volume of a sphere:

V = (4/3)πr^3

Where V is the volume of the sphere, π is pi (approximately 3.14), and r is the radius of the sphere. Since we are given the diameter of the sphere, we need to divide it by 2 to get the radius:

r = d/2 = 3/2 = 1.5 cm

Now we can calculate the volume of the sphere with 54 rubber bands:

V1 = (4/3)π(1.5)^3 = 14.14 cm^3

To increase the diameter of the sphere by 1 cm, we need to increase the radius by 0.5 cm. The new radius is:

r' = r + 0.5 = 2 cm

We can now calculate the new volume of the sphere using the same formula:

V2 = (4/3)π(2)^3 = 33.51 cm^3

To find how many rubber bands Alana should add to the ball to increase the diameter by 1 cm, we need to calculate the difference between the two volumes:

ΔV = V2 - V1 = 19.37 cm^3

Since we know that all the rubber bands have the same volume, we will divide the change in volume by the volume of one rubber band:

ΔV / V_rubber_band = number of rubber bands

We do not know the volume of a single rubber band, but we know that 54 rubber bands make up the original sphere, so we can divide the volume of the original sphere by 54 to get the volume of one rubber band:

V1 / 54 = volume of one rubber band

Now we can substitute this expression into our formula:

ΔV / (V1 / 54) = number of rubber bands to add

Plugging in the numbers, we get:

ΔV / (V1 / 54) = 19.37 / (14.14 / 54) ≈ 42 rubber bands

Therefore, Alana should add approximately 42 rubber bands to the ball to increase its diameter by 1 cm.

Sure! 

Since the given isosceles triangle has two sides of length 15 cm and 10 cm, we know that these two sides are the “legs” of the triangle, and that the third side (the “base”) must have length somewhere between 10 cm and 15 cm in order for the triangle to exist. 

To maximize the perimeter of the triangle, we want to choose the longest possible length for the base. Since the triangle is isosceles, we know that the base will also have length 15 cm if it is as long as possible, because the two legs have equal length. 

So the perimeter of the triangle is: 

Perimeter = 15 cm + 15 cm + 10 cm 

Perimeter = 40 cm 

Therefore, the greatest possible perimeter of the given isosceles triangle is 40 cm.

Let the radius be $r$ and the height be $h$. Since the lateral surface area is 3.5 square inches, we have $2\pi rh=3.5$. Since the volume is 3.5 cubic inches, we have $\pi r^2h=3.5$. Dividing the latter equation by the former, we find that $r=\boxed{2}$ inches.

Sure! To solve this problem, we need to use the formulas for the lateral surface area and volume of a right circular cylinder. 

Let $r$ be the radius of the cylinder and $h$ be the height. Then we have:

Lateral surface area = $2\pi rh = 3.5$ square inches 

Volume = $\pi r^2h = 3.5$ cubic inches 

We can use the volume formula to solve for $h$:

$h = \frac{3.5}{\pi r^2}$ 

Substituting this into the lateral surface area formula, we get:

$2\pi r \cdot \frac{3.5}{\pi r^2} = 3.5$ 

Simplifying this equation gives:

$\frac{7}{r} = 1$ 

Therefore, the radius of the cylinder is $r = 7$ inches.

In its initial position, suppose the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $X$, with point $P$ directly above $X$ on the top line. Consider when the semi-circle rocks to the right. [asy]
size(10cm);

// Variables
path semicircle = (-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{left}(0, -8){left}..{up}(-8, 0);
real xy = 4 * pi / 3;
pair x = (0, -8); pair p = (0, 4);
pair o = (xy, 0); pair z = (xy, 4); pair y = (xy, -8);

// Drawing
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));
draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));
draw(semicircle, dashed);
draw(x--p, dashed);
draw(shift(xy) * rotate(-30) * semicircle);
draw(z--y);

// labels
label("$Q$", (-4 * sqrt(3) + xy, 4), N);
label("$P$", (0, 4), N);
label("$Z$", (xy, 4), N);
label("$O$", (xy, 0), NE);
label("$X$", (0, -8), S);
label("$Y$", (xy, -8), S);
[/asy] Suppose now the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $Y$ (with $O$ the point on the top of the semi-circle directly above $Y$, and $Z$ the point on the top line directly above $Y$) and touches the top line at $Q$.  Note that $XY=PZ$.

$Q$ is one of the desired points where the semi-circle touches the line above.  Because the diagram is symmetrical, the other point will be the mirror image of $Q$ in line $XP$.  Thus, the required distance is 2 times the length of $PQ$.

Now $PQ=QZ-PZ = QZ-XY$. Since the semi-circle is tangent to the bottom line, and $YO$ is perpendicular to the bottom line and $O$ lies on a diameter, we know that $O$ is the centre of the circle. So $OY=OQ= 8$ cm, since both are radii (or since the centre always lies on a line parallel to the bottom line and a distance of the radius away).

Also, $OZ=4$ cm, since the distance between the two lines is 12 cm. By the Pythagorean Theorem (since $\angle QZO=90^\circ$), then \[ QZ^2 = QO^2 - ZO^2 = 8^2 - 4^2 = 64 - 16 =48\]so $QZ = 4\sqrt{3}$ cm.

Also, since $QZ:ZO = \sqrt{3}:1$, then $\angle QOZ = 60^\circ$.

Thus, the angle from $QO$ to the horizontal is $30^\circ$, so the semi-circle has rocked through an angle of $30^\circ$, ie. has rocked through $\frac{1}{12}$ of a full revolution (if it was a full circle). Thus, the distance of $Y$ from $X$ is $\frac{1}{12}$ of the circumference of what would be the full circle of radius 8, or $XY=\frac{1}{12}(2\pi(8))=\frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.  (We can think of a wheel turning through $30^\circ$ and the related horizontal distance through which it travels.)

Thus, $PQ = QZ-XY = 4\sqrt{3} - \frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.

Therefore, the required distance is double this, or $8\sqrt{3}-\frac{8}{3}\pi$ cm or about 5.4788 cm, which is closest to $\boxed{55}$ mm.

Sure, let's work through this problem step by step.

First, we need to visualize the problem. We can draw a diagram of the semi-circle and the two lines it bounces between. The distance between the lines is 12 cm, so we can label this on our diagram. We can also label the radius of the semi-circle, which is 8 cm.

[asy]

draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));draw((-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{up}(-8, 0));
draw(arc((0,0),8,0,180),dotted);

[/asy]

Next, we need to find the distance between the two points where the semi-circle touches the line above. To do this, we can draw a line that passes through the center of the circle and intersects the line above at a right angle, creating a right triangle.

[asy]

draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));draw((-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{up}(-8, 0));
draw(arc((0,0),8,0,180),dotted);
draw((0,0)--(0,12)--(-8,12),dashed);

[/asy]

We know the radius of the circle is 8 cm, and the distance between the two lines is 12 cm. We can label the length of the line we just drew as $h$. We can also use the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of the base of the right triangle, which we'll call $x$.

\begin{align*}
x^2 + h^2 &= (2r)^2 \\
x^2 + 12^2 &= (2 \cdot 8)^2 \\
x^2 + 144 &= 256 \\
x^2 &= 112 \\
x &= \sqrt{112} \approx 10.6\text{ cm}
\end{align*}

So the distance between the two points where the semi-circle touches the line above is twice this length, or approximately 21.2 cm. To convert this to millimeters and round to the nearest whole number, we can multiply by 10 and round.

$$ 21.2 \text{ cm} \cdot 10 = 212 \text{ mm} \approx \boxed{212} $$

Therefore, the distance between the two points where the semi-circle touches the line above is approximately 212 millimeters.

Since $AC$ and $BD$ are line segments that intersect at point $O$, angle $AOD$ and angle $AOB$ are supplementary angles and their angle measures must add up to $180$ degrees. Since angle $AOD$ measures $54$ degrees, the measure of angle $AOB$ must be $180 - 54 = \boxed{126}$ degrees.

[/asy]

Now, we know that $AOD$ measures $54$ degrees, and we can see that it is made up of two congruent $36$ degree angles (since $\angle AOD = \angle COD$ and $\angle COB = \angle AOB$ as vertical angles). This means that $\angle BOA = 180 - (\angle AOD) = 126$ degrees.

We can use this fact along with some basic properties of triangles to find $\angle AOB$. Let $x$ be the measure of $\angle AOB$. Then, we have

\begin{align*}
\angle AOC &= 2x \\
\angle BOD &= 2(180 - x) = 360 - 2x \\
\angle AOD &= \angle AOC + \angle BOD \\
54 &= 2x + (360 - 2x) \\
54 &= 360 - x \\
x &= \boxed{306}.
\end{align*}

So $\angle AOB$ measures $306$ degrees.

Let $P$ be the point on the unit circle that is $45^\circ$ counterclockwise from $(1,0)$, and let $D$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to the $x$-axis, as shown below.

Sure, I can help you with that! 

We know that $\tan 45^\circ$ is the ratio of the length of the side opposite to $45^\circ$ in a right-angled triangle to the length of the side adjacent to $45^\circ$ in the same triangle.

We can draw a triangle with a $45^\circ$ angle and the other two angles equal to $45^\circ$ also. Since the triangle is isosceles, its two equal sides are congruent. 

            /|
           / |
    sqrt(2)/  |  <- opposite leg and adjacent leg are the same length since the other two angles are also equal to 45 degrees
         /   |
        /____|
       sqrt(2)

In this triangle, we can see that the opposite and adjacent lengths are both $\sqrt{2}$. Thus, 

$$\tan 45^\circ = \frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{adjacent}} = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{\sqrt{2}} = 1$$

Therefore, $\tan 45^\circ = 1$.

To find the area of triangle $ADE$, we need to find the base and height of the triangle. The base is $DE$, which has length $6-2=4$. To find the height, note that $AD$ is the side of the rectangle parallel to $DE$, so the height of the triangle is the same as the height of the rectangle, which is 10. Therefore, the area of triangle $ADE$ is $\frac{1}{2}(4)(10)=20$.

To find the area of quadrilateral $ABCE$, we can split it into two triangles, $ABC$ and $ECB$. The area of triangle $ABC$ is $\frac{1}{2}(6)(10)=30$. To find the area of triangle $ECB$, we need to find the length of $EB$. Note that $CD$ is a vertical segment, and $E$ is on $CD$ at $(2, 10)$, so $E$ is 4 units away from $C$ horizontally. Since $C$ has coordinates $(6, 10)$, this means that $E$ has coordinates $(6-4, 10) = (2, 10)$. Therefore, $EB=2$, and the area of triangle $ECB$ is $\frac{1}{2}(2)(10) = 10$. The total area of quadrilateral $ABCE$ is then $30+10=40$.

Therefore, the ratio of the area of triangle $ADE$ to the area of quadrilateral $ABCE$ is $\frac{20}{40} = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}$.

We know that $OA$ and $OB$ are each radii of the semi-circle with center $O$. Thus, $OA=OB=OC+CB=32+36=68$. Therefore, $AC=AO+OC=68+32=100$.

The semi-circle with center $K$ has radius $AK=\frac{1}{2}(AC)=\frac{1}{2}(100)=50$.

The shaded area is equal to the area of the largest semi-circle with center $O$, minus the combined areas of the two smaller unshaded semi-circles with centers $K$ and $M$.  The radius of the smaller unshaded circle is $MB=\frac{1}{2}(CB)=\frac{1}{2}(36)=18$.  Therefore, the shaded area equals \begin{align*}
&\frac{1}{2}\pi(OB)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(AK)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(MB)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(50)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(18)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68^2-50^2-18^2)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(4624-2500-324)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(1800)\\
&=\boxed{900\pi}
\end{align*}

We can begin by drawing some triangles and noticing their relationships. For example, we can draw the heights of the triangles with bases on $AM$, $OC$, and $BK$ respectively, which are all perpendicular to line segment $AB$: [asy]
pair A, K, O, C, M, B, X, Y, Z, H1, H2, H3;
O=(0,0);
C=(32,0);
M=(50,0);
B=(68,0);
A=(-68,0);
K=(A+C)/2;
X=(0,68);
Y=(-18,50);
Z=(50,18);
path nom, bigc, middlec, smallc;
nom=A--B--(100,100)--(-100,100)--cycle;
bigc=A..X..B--cycle;
middlec=A..Y..C--cycle;
smallc=C..Z..B--cycle;
fill(bigc, gray(.5));
fill(middlec, white);
fill(smallc, white);
draw(smallc);
draw(middlec);
draw(bigc);
draw(A--B);
H1=(A+C)/2;
H2=foot(O, A, M);
H3=foot(B,K,C);
draw(A--H1);
draw(M--H2);
draw(K--H3);
label("$h_1$", (O+H2)/2, NW);
label("$h_2$", (H3+O)/2, E);
label("$h_3$", (H1+K)/2, S);
[/asy] Then, we can recognize that $\triangle ACO$ is a right triangle with legs of length $32$ and $AK/2$. We can apply the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of $AK$:

\begin{align*}
AK^2 &= AO^2 - OK^2 \\
&= (2CM)^2 - \left(\frac{AB}{2}\right)^2 \\
&= 4CM^2 - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(OC^2 + CM^2) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(32^2 + (CM+BM)^2) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(32^2 + (CM+68)^2) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(32^2 + (BM+49)^2) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(32^2 + 49^2 + BM^2 + 2\cdot 49\cdot BM) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(32^2 + 49^2 + (s-CO)^2 + 2\cdot 49(s-CO)) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(32^2+49^2+(s-32)^2+2\cdot 49(s-32)) - \frac{AB^2}{4} \\
&= 4(s^2 + 2896) - \frac{(2s)^2}{4} \\
&= 12s^2 - 2s^2 \\
&= 10s^2 
\end{align*}

where we used the fact that $CO=s-32$ and $BM=49$, and defined $s$ as the length of the segment $AB$. We can then use the formula for the area of a triangle, $\frac{1}{2}bh$, to find the area of the shaded region in terms of $s$:

$$\frac{1}{2}CO\cdot AB + \frac{1}{2}AK\cdot OB + \frac{1}{2}BK\cdot OC$$

Substituting $CO=s-32$, $AK=s$, $OB=s$, and $BK=s-36$, we get:

\begin{align*}
\frac{1}{2}(s-32)s + \frac{1}{2} s\cdot s + \frac{1}{2}(s-36)\cdot 32 &= \frac{1}{2}(s^2 - 32s + s^2 + 16s + 16s - 576) \\
&= \frac{1}{2}(2s^2 + 31s - 576) \\
&= s^2 + \frac{31}{2} s - 288
\end{align*}

Thus, the area of the shaded region is $\boxed{s^2 + \frac{31}{2} s - 288}$ square units. We can check this answer by plugging in the given values for $CO$ and $CB$ and solving for $s$, then verifying that the answer matches the actual area of the shaded region.

Yes, sure! Let's solve this math problem. 

To calculate the area of the circle we need to use the formula $A = \pi r^2$, where $A$ is the area of the circle and $r$ is the radius of the circle. 

Looking at the diagram, we see that the center of the circle is at the point $C(3,1)$, and another point ($X$) on the circle is $(8,1)$. Thus, the radius of the circle is the distance between point $C(3,1)$ and $(8,1)$, which is $r=\sqrt{(8-3)^2 + (1-1)^2}=5$. 

Plugging $r=5$ into the formula for the area of a circle gives us: 

$$ A = \pi r^2 = \pi \cdot 5^2 = 25\pi $$

So the area of the circle is $25\pi$. 

I hope that helps! Let me know if you have any questions or if there's anything else I can do for you.

[asy]

Let $\overline{PQ}$ be a line segment in set $\cal
S$ that is not a side of the square, and let $M$ be the midpoint of $\overline{PQ}$. Let $A$ be the vertex of the square that is on both the side that contains $P$ and the side that contains $Q$. Because $\overline{AM}$ is the median to the hypotenuse of right $\triangle PAQ$, $AM=(1/2)\cdot PQ=(1/2)\cdot2=1$.  Thus every midpoint is 1 unit from a vertex of the square, and the set of all the midpoints forms four quarter-circles of radius 1 and with centers at the vertices of the square.  The area of the region bounded by the four arcs is $4-4\cdot(\pi/4)=4-\pi$, so $100k=100(4-3.14)=\boxed{86}$.

$$\centerline{{\bf OR}}$$Place a coordinate system so that the vertices of the square are at $(0,0)$, $(2,0)$, $(2,2)$, and $(0,2)$.  When the segment's vertices are on the sides that contain $(0,0)$, its endpoints' coordinates can be represented as $(a,0)$ and $(0,b)$.  Let the coordinates of the midpoint of the segment be $(x,y)$.  Then $(x,y)=(a/2,b/2)$ and $a^2+b^2=4$.  Thus $x^2+y^2=(a/2)^2+(b/2)^2
= 1$, and the midpoints of these segments form a quarter-circle with radius 1 centered at the origin.  The set of all the midpoints forms four quarter-circles, and the area of the region bounded by the four arcs is $4-4\cdot(\pi/4)=4-\pi$, so $100k=100(4-3.14)=\boxed{86}$.

Sure, I'd be happy to help! 

Let's start by drawing a square of side length 2 and labeling its vertices A, B, C, and D. We can think of the line segments in set S as connecting adjacent vertices of the square, such as AB, BC, CD, and DA.

[asy]
unitsize(1cm);
draw((0,0)--(2,0)--(2,2)--(0,2)--cycle);
label("A", (0,0), SW);
label("B", (2,0), SE);
label("C", (2,2), NE);
label("D", (0,2), NW);
draw((0,1)--(2,1), red+linewidth(1));
draw((1,0)--(1,2), red+linewidth(1));
label("$2$",(0.5,0),S);
label("$2$",(1,1.5),E);
[/asy]

The midpoints of each line segment in set S will form a square, as shown in red below. Since each line segment in set S has length 2, the length of each side of the smaller square is 1.

[asy]
unitsize(1cm);
draw((0,0)--(2,0)--(2,2)--(0,2)--cycle);
label("A", (0,0), SW);
label("B", (2,0), SE);
label("C", (2,2), NE);
label("D", (0,2), NW);
draw((0,1)--(2,1), red+linewidth(1));
draw((1,0)--(1,2), red+linewidth(1));
label("$2$",(0.5,0),S);
label("$2$",(1,1.5),E);
draw((0.5,0)--(0.5,2)--(1.5,2)--(1.5,0)--cycle, red+linewidth(1));
draw((0.5,0.5)--(1.5,0.5), blue+linewidth(1));
draw((0.5,1.5)--(1.5,1.5), blue+linewidth(1));
label("$1$",(1,1),NW);

Let $r$ and $h$ denote the radius of the semicircle and the height of the triangle respectively. Let point $O$ divide hypotenuse $\overline{AB}$ into segments of length $x$ and $y$ so that $AO = OB = r$.

Then, because $AOB$ is a right triangle, $x^2 + y^2 = (2r)^2 = 4r^2$.

Also, note that the semicircle has radius $r$ and area $2\pi$, so its diameter has length $2r\sqrt{2\pi}$ and its circumference has length $2\pi r\sqrt{2\pi}$. Therefore, we must have $h = 2r\sqrt{2\pi}$ and $c = 2\pi r\sqrt{2\pi}$.

Using the Pythagorean Theorem on the right triangles $ACO$ and $BCO$ gives us $x^2 + h^2 = r^2$ and $y^2 + h^2 = r^2$, so adding these equations together gives $x^2 + y^2 + 2h^2 = 2r^2$. Substituting the expressions we found for $h$, $r$, and $x^2 + y^2$ gives us:

$$4r^2\pi + 8r^2 = 8r^2\pi$$

Simplifying this equation gives $r^2 = \frac{4}{\pi - 2}$, and substituting this expression into the equation we found for the area of triangle $ABC$, $A = \frac{1}{2}xy$, gives:

$$A = \frac{1}{2}xy = \frac{1}{2}\cdot\frac{1}{2}(4r^2 - h^2) = \boxed{4\pi - 8\sqrt{2}\pi}$$

We know that $\tan$ has period $180^\circ$, meaning that $\tan (180^\circ + n \cdot 180^\circ) = \tan 180^\circ$ for any integer $n$. Since $\tan 180^\circ$ is undefined (this is because $\tan \theta = \frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta}$, and $\cos 180^\circ = -1$), we conclude that $\boxed{\text{undefined}}$ is the answer.

1. The original cube has a side of 3 inches. So, the volume of the cube is 3 x 3 x 3 = 27 cubic inches.

2. A cube of side 1 inch is cut from each corner. The new side length of the cube will be 3 - 2 = 1 inch.

3. The volume of each small cube inserted in the corner is 2 x 2 x 2 = 8 cubic inches. Since there are 8 corners, the total volume of the small cubes is 8 x 8 = 64 cubic inches.

4. The resulting solid is made up of the original cube minus the small cubes, plus the new cubes. So, the volume of the resulting solid is 27 - 64 + 8 x 8 = 27 - 64 + 64 = 27 cubic inches.

5. To find the surface area of the resulting solid, we need to find the surface area of the original cube and subtract the surface area of the small cubes and add the surface area of the new cubes.

6. The surface area of the original cube is 6 x 3 x 3 = 54 square inches.

7. The surface area of each small cube is 6 x 1 x 1 = 6 square inches. Since there are 8 small cubes, the total surface area of the small cubes is 8 x 6 = 48 square inches.

8. The surface area of each new cube is 6 x 2 x 2 = 24 square inches. Since there are 8 new cubes, the total surface area of the new cubes is 8 x 24 = 192 square inches.

9. So, the surface area of the resulting solid is 54 - 48 + 192 = 198 square inches.

Therefore, the number of square inches in the surface area of the resulting solid is 198.

The measure of an interior angle in a regular pentagon is  $$\frac{180(5-2)}{5}=108^{\circ},$$ so $\angle QPT = 108^\circ$.  From isosceles triangle $PQT$, we have $\angle PQT = (180^\circ - \angle QPT)/2 = 36^\circ$.  Similarly, $\angle RQS = 36^\circ$.  Finally, $\triangle SQT$ is isosceles with $SQ=QT$, so median $\overline{QX}$ is also an angle bisector of $\angle SQT$.  Since $\angle SQT = \angle PQR - 36^\circ-36^\circ = 108^\circ-36^\circ-36^\circ = 36^\circ$, we have $\angle XQS = (\angle SQT)/2 = \boxed{18^\circ}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We know that for a regular pentagon, each interior angle measure is 108 degrees. Thus, $PQS$ and $TQR$ are congruent triangles (when paired with $Q$ that is). So, we know that $\angle QTS$ also measures $108$ degrees. Since $X$ is the midpoint of $TS$, $XQ$ is an angle bisector of $\angle QTS.$ Thus, $\angle QTX$ and $\angle STX$ are half of $108,$ or $54$ degrees each. 

Therefore, we have \begin{align*}
\angle XQS &= \angle QTS - \angle QTX - \angle STX \\
&= 108^\circ - 54^\circ - 54^\circ \\
&= \boxed{54^\circ}.
\end{align*}

Because of our parallel bases, we can see that corresponding angles of the triangles must be congruent. Therefore, by AA similarity, we see that the two triangles are similar.

If two similar triangles have side ratios of $r : 1,$ the ratio of their areas must be $r^2 : 1.$ In our diagram, we see that the ratio of the sides of the smaller triangle to the sides of the larger triangle is $\dfrac{4\text{ cm}}{10\text{ cm}} = \dfrac{2}{5}.$ Therefore, the ratio of the areas is the square of that, or $\left(\dfrac{2}{5}\right)^2 = \boxed{\dfrac{4}{25}}.$

Sure, I can help you with that! 
Let's label the vertices of the larger triangle as $A$, $B$, and $C$, with $AB$ and $BC$ as the parallel sides. We can then label the vertices of the smaller triangle as $A'$, $B'$, and $C$, with $A'B'$ parallel to $AB$ and $B'C$ parallel to $BC$. Let's also label the length of $AB$ as $x$ and the length of $BC$ as $y$.

Since $A'B'$ is parallel to $AB$, we know by similar triangles that $\dfrac{A'B'}{AB} = \dfrac{1}{3}$. Similarly, we have $\dfrac{B'C}{BC} = \dfrac{2}{3}$. 

We can use these ratios to find the length of $A'C$. Let's call the length of $A'C$ as $z$. Then, we have:

$A'B' + B'C = A'C$
$\dfrac{1}{3}x + \dfrac{2}{3}y = z$

Now, we can use the formula for the area of a triangle, $\dfrac{1}{2}bh$, where $b$ is the length of the base and $h$ is the height of the triangle. The height of each triangle is the altitude drawn to the base, so we need to find the lengths of those altitudes.

Let's call the altitude for triangle $ABC$ as $h_1$. We can use the fact that the area of a triangle is equal to $\dfrac{1}{2}bh$ to find $h_1$. Then, we have:

$\text{Area of } \Delta ABC = \dfrac{1}{2}(x)(h_1) = 20$
$h_1 = \dfrac{40}{x}$

Similarly, let's call the altitude for triangle $A'B'C$ as $h_2$. We can use the fact that the area of a triangle is equal to $\dfrac{1}{2}bh$ to find $h_2$. Then, we have:

$\text{Area of } \Delta A'B'C = \dfrac{1}{2}\left(\dfrac{1}{3}x\right)(h_2) = \dfrac{1}{6}(x)(h_2) = k$
$h_2 = \dfrac{6k}{x}$

Now we can find the area of triangle $A'B'C$ in terms of $x$ and $y$. The area of triangle $A'B'C$ is equal to $\dfrac{1}{2}(A'C)(h_2)$. Plugging in the values we found earlier, we have:

$\text{Area of } \Delta A'B'C = \dfrac{1}{2}\left(\dfrac{1}{3}x+\dfrac{2}{3}y\right)\left(\dfrac{6k}{x}\right) = ky$

The ratio of the area of triangle $A'B'C$ to the area of triangle $ABC$ is then:

$\dfrac{\text{Area of } \Delta A'B'C}{\text{Area of } \Delta ABC} = \dfrac{ky}{20} = \dfrac{1}{3}$

So the ratio of the area of the smaller triangle to the area of the larger triangle is $\boxed{\dfrac{1}{3}}$.

Sure, I can help you with that problem from precalculus!

First, we need to use the formula for the volume of a cone, which is:

$V = \dfrac{1}{3} \pi r^2 h$

where $r$ is the radius of the base and $h$ is the height of the cone.

We're given the radius $r = 3$ inches and the volume $V = 30 \pi$ cubic inches. Plugging in these values, we get:

$30 \pi = \dfrac{1}{3} \pi (3^2) h$

Simplifying:

$30 \pi = 9 \pi h$

Dividing both sides by $9 \pi$:

$h = \dfrac{30}{9} = \dfrac{10}{3} \approx 3.33$ inches

So the cup will need to be approximately 3.33 inches tall to hold 30$\pi$ cubic inches of water.

An $8-15-17$ triangle is always right. It follows that the hypotenuse is a diameter of the circle, and the radius must be half the hypotenuse, or $\frac{17}{2} = \boxed{8.5}$.

Sure! In a triangle, the circumcenter is the center of the circle that circumscribes the triangle. Since the circumcenter is equidistant from the vertices of the triangle, it must lie on the perpendicular bisectors of the sides.

Let's start by finding the lengths of the perpendicular bisectors of the sides. Using the Pythagorean Theorem, we find that the lengths of the sides squared are 64, 225 and 289. The perimeter is 8+15+17 = 40, so the semiperimeter is half of that, or 20. We can use Heron's formula to find the area of the triangle:

sqrt(20(20-8)(20-15)(20-17)) = sqrt(20*12*5*3) = 2*2*sqrt(15)

The area of a triangle can also be expressed as (abc)/(4R), where a, b and c are the lengths of the sides, and R is the radius of the circumcircle. Plugging in the values we have, we get:

(8*15*17)/(4R) = 2*2*sqrt(15)

Simplifying, we get:

255/R = 4sqrt(15)
R = 255/(4sqrt(15)) ≈ 16.4

So the radius of the circle is approximately 16.4 units.

Extracting Answer from: 
Join $PQ$, $PR$, $PS$, $RQ$, and $RS$. Since the circles with center $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are all tangent to $BC$, then $QR$ and $RS$ are each parallel to $BC$ (as the centres $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are each 1 unit above $BC$). This tells us that $QS$ passes through $R$. When the centers of tangent circles are joined, the line segments formed pass through the associated point of tangency, and so have lengths equal to the sum of the radii of those circles. Therefore, $QR=RS=PR=PS=1+1=2$.

[asy]
size(200);
pair P, Q, R, S;
Q=(0,0);
R=(2,0);
S=(4,0);
P=(3,1.732);
label("Q", Q, SW);
label("R", R, dir(270));
label("S", S, SE);
label("P", P, N);
draw(circle(Q,1), dashed);
draw(circle(P,1), dashed);
draw(circle(R,1), dashed);
draw(circle(S,1), dashed);
draw(P--Q--S--P--R);
[/asy]

Since $PR=PS=RS$, we know $\triangle PRS$ is equilateral, so $\angle PSR=\angle PRS=60^\circ$.  Since $\angle PRS=60^\circ$ and $QRS$ is a straight line, we have $\angle QRP=180^\circ-60^\circ=120^\circ$. Since $QR=RP$, we know $\triangle QRP$ is isosceles, so $$\angle PQR = \frac{1}{2}(180^\circ-120^\circ)= 30^\circ.$$Since $\angle PQS=30^\circ$ and $\angle PSQ=60^\circ$, we have $\angle QPS = 180^\circ - 30^\circ - 60^\circ = 90^\circ$, so $\triangle PQS$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Thus, the answer is $\boxed{30^\circ}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly, let $\angle PQS=2 \alpha.$ Since $PS=PQ=1,$ we calculate $QS = 2 \cos(2 \alpha)\,$ and $[QS] = QS \cdot RS \cdot \sin(2 \alpha) = 2 \cdot 2 \cdot \sin(\alpha) \cdot \cos(\alpha)$ so that $[QRS] = 2 \sin(\alpha) \cdot \cos(\alpha).$

Now, we have either $\angle PRS = 180^{\circ} - 2 \alpha$ or $\angle PRS=3 \alpha$ so that $RS=2 \sin(2 \alpha)$ or $RS=2 \sin(3 \alpha).$ Therefore, we calculate $[PRS] = \frac{1}{2} \cdot PR \cdot RS \cdot \sin(120^{\circ}) = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 2 \cdot RS \cdot \sin(120^{\circ}) = RS \cdot \sqrt{3}$ so that $[QRS] = [PRS].$

Equating the two expressions for $[QRS]$ we just found, we have $2\sin(\alpha)\cos(\alpha) = RS\sqrt{3} = 2\sin(\alpha) \cos(\alpha) + \sin(\alpha) \cos(\alpha).$ Hence $\sin(\alpha)\cos(\alpha)=\sin(\alpha)\cos(\alpha)+(1/2) \sin(\alpha) \cos(\alpha)$ implying $1=(1/2) \sin(\alpha)$ which has $\sin(\alpha)=2.$

Therefore, $\angle PQS = 2 \alpha = \sin^{-1}(2).$ So the measure of the smallest angle in triangle $PQS$ is $\boxed{74}.$

Let $P$ be the point on the unit circle that is $330^\circ$ counterclockwise from $(1,0)$, and let $D$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to the $x$-axis, as shown below.

[asy]

Sure, I'd be happy to help you with that math problem! 

Let's start by drawing a picture of the situation. We have a sphere inscribed in a cube, which means its diameter is equal to the side length of the cube. 

[Insert image of a cube with a sphere inscribed inside it]

The volume of the sphere can be expressed as $V_{sphere} = \frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$ where $r$ is the radius of the sphere. Since the sphere is inscribed in a cube, we know that $d = 2r$ (where $d$ is the diameter of the sphere and equal to the side length of the cube). Therefore, we can rewrite the volume of the sphere as $V_{sphere} = \frac{4}{3}\pi\left(\frac{d}{2}\right)^3 = \frac{\pi}{6}$. 

Simplifying, we get:

$$\frac{4}{3}\pi\left(\frac{d}{2}\right)^3 = \frac{\pi}{6}$$

$$\Rightarrow \left(\frac{d}{2}\right)^3 = \frac{1}{8}$$

$$\Rightarrow \frac{d}{2} = \frac{1}{2}$$

$$\Rightarrow d = 1$$

So the diameter of the sphere (and the side length of the cube) is 1 inch. 

Therefore, the volume of the cube is simply $V_{cube} = s^3 = 1^3 = \boxed{1}$ cubic inch.

Let the two different sides of the rectangle be $a$ and $b$. Since the perimeter is 26 units, we have the equation $2a+2b=26\Rightarrow a+b=13$. We can rearrange this equation to get $a=13-b$. We want to minimize the value of $\sqrt{a^2+b^2}$. Substituting in the last equation, we have $\sqrt{(13-b)^2+b^2}=\sqrt{169-26b+2b^2}$. This value is minimized when the quadratic $169-26b+2b^2$ is minimized, which occurs when $b$ (which must be an integer) is as close to $-\frac{-26}{2\cdot2}=6.5$ as possible. So let $b=7$ and $a=13-7=6$. So, the shortest possible diagonal is $\sqrt{7^2+6^2}=\boxed{\sqrt{85}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure! Let's work on this problem together.

Let the length of the rectangle be x units and the width of the rectangle be y units. Since the perimeter of the rectangle is 26 units, we have:

2x + 2y = 26

Simplifying this equation, we get:

x + y = 13

We are given that the side lengths of the rectangle are integers, so x and y must be integers. We want to find the length of the shortest possible diagonal, which is the hypotenuse of a right triangle formed by the sides of the rectangle.

We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of the diagonal:

d^2 = x^2 + y^2

We want to minimize d, which means we want to minimize d^2. Substituting y = 13 - x into the equation above and simplifying, we get:

d^2 = x^2 + (13 - x)^2
d^2 = 2x^2 - 26x + 169

To minimize d^2, we need to find the value of x that minimizes this quadratic expression. We can do this by finding the x-value where the derivative of the expression is equal to zero.

Taking the derivative of the expression with respect to x, we get:

d/dx (d^2) = 4x - 26

Setting this expression equal to zero and solving for x, we get:

4x - 26 = 0
x = 6.5

Since x must be an integer, we round x up to 7. Then, we can calculate y = 13 - x = 6.

Finally, we can calculate the length of the diagonal using the Pythagorean theorem:

d^2 = 7^2 + 6^2
d^2 = 85
d = sqrt(85)

Therefore, the length of the shortest possible diagonal is sqrt(85) units, expressed in simplest radical form.

Extracting Answer from: 
Let $s$ be the side length of the square inscribed in triangle $ABC$ and $s'$ be the side length of the square inscribed in triangle $DEF$. Drawing altitudes from the hypotenuse of each triangle upon a perpendicular bisector of that hypotenuse, we have $s=\tfrac{1}{2}(c-b)$ and $s'=\tfrac{1}{2}(f-e)$, where the hypotenuse of triangle $ABC$ is $BC$ and that of triangle $DEF$ is $EF$. As $ABC$ and $DEF$ are congruent, isosceles right triangles, we have $AC=AB=\sqrt{2}BC$ and $DF=DE=\sqrt{2}EF$, so $2b^{2} = c^{2}$ and $2e^{2} = f^{2}$. Moreover, we have $s^{2}=bc-15$ and $s'^{2}=ef$. From this, we get that $$s = \tfrac{1}{2}(c-b) = \tfrac{1}{2}\cdot\frac{c^{2}-b^{2}}{c-b} = \tfrac{1}{2}\cdot\frac{(2b^{2}+b^{2})-(2b^{2})}{c-b} = \tfrac{1}{2}b,$$so $s=b/2$. Similarly, we get $s'=e/2$. Now, we have that \begin{align*}
s'^{2}&=\left(\tfrac{1}{2}e\right)^{2}=\frac{e^{2}}{4}=\frac{(2b^{2}/2)^{2}}{4} = \boxed{\frac{b^{4}}{16}}.
\end{align*}

To save the most rope, we must have $HP$ having minimum length.
For $HP$ to have minimum length, $HP$ must be perpendicular to $CD$. [asy]
pair C, D, H, P;
H=(90,120);
C=(0,0);
D=(140,0);
P=(90,0);
draw(H--C--D--H--P);
label("H", H, N);
label("C", C, SW);
label("D", D, SE);
label("P", P, S);
label("150", (C+H)/2, NW);
label("130", (D+H)/2, NE);
[/asy] (Among other things, we can see from this diagram that sliding $P$ away from the perpendicular position does make $HP$ longer.)
In the diagram, $HC=150$, $HD=130$ and $CD=140$.
Let $HP=x$ and $PD=a$.  Then $CP=140-a$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPC$, $x^2 + (140-a)^2 = 150^2$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPD$, $x^2+a^2 = 130^2$.
Subtracting the second equation from the first, we obtain \begin{align*}
(140-a)^2 - a^2 & = 150^2 - 130^2 \\
(19600 - 280a+a^2)-a^2 & = 5600 \\
19600 -280a & = 5600 \\
280a & = 14000 \\
a & = 50
\end{align*} Therefore, $x^2 + 90^2 = 150^2$ or $x^2 = 150^2 - 90^2 = 22500 - 8100 = 14400$ so $x =120$.
So the shortest possible rope that we can use is 120 m, which saves $130+150-120 = \boxed{160}$ m of rope.

Extracting Answer from: 
Hi! This problem involves basic concepts of geometry and algebra. 
Firstly, let's observe the position of $O$ on the plane. $A$ is directly north of $O$, and $C$ is directly south of $O$, so $\overline{AC}$ passes through $O$. Similarly $\overline{BD}$ passes through $O$. 
Let the length of $\overline{OP}$ = $x$, so the length of $\overline{CP}$ is $140 - x$. Using the Pythagorean theorem, we can express $OH^2$, $HA^2$, $HP^2$, $HB^2$, $HC^2$ and $HD^2$ in terms of $x$. 

$OH^2 = HA^2 - OA^2 = HA^2 - HC^2 = (150-x)^2 - 10000$

$HP^2 = HC^2 + CP^2 = HC^2 + (140-x)^2$

$HB^2 = HP^2 + PB^2 = HP^2 + OB^2 = HP^2 + 10000$

$HD^2 = HB^2 - BD^2 = HB^2 - 19600 = HP^2 + 10000 - 19600$

We want to minimize the length of the ropes, i.e. minimize $HA + HB + HC + HD$. Substituting the expressions above we get:

$(150-x) + \sqrt{(150-x)^2 + (140-x)^2} + \sqrt{(140-x)^2 + (10000+x^2)} + \sqrt{(10000-2x)^2 + (140-x)^2}$

We can minimize the above expression using calculus, namely by finding its derivative and setting it equal to 0.

$\frac{dL}{dx} = \frac{2(150-x)+2(140-x)\cdot(-1/\sqrt{(150-x)^2 + (140-x)^2})+2(140-x)\cdot(-1/\sqrt{(140-x)^2 + (10000+x^2)})+2(10000-2x)\cdot(-1/\sqrt{(10000-2x)^2 + (140-x)^2})}{dx} = 0$

$\Rightarrow (150-x) + \frac{(140-x)}{\sqrt{(150-x)^2 + (140-x)^2}} + \frac{(140-x)}{\sqrt{(140-x)^2 + (10000+x^2)}} + \frac{(10000-2x)}{\sqrt{(10000-2x)^2 + (140-x)^2}} = 0$

Unfortunately this doesn't simplify algebraically, so we'll have to use numerical methods (e.g. Newton's method) to approximate $x$. Once we find the value of $x$ that minimizes the total rope length, we can substitute this value in the expression for $HP$ to find the length of the single rope that replaces $HC$ and $HD$. The difference between the sum of the original rope lengths and the length of the new rope is the greatest amount of rope that can be saved.
 
I hope that helps! Good luck with precalculus!

The two bases are octagons, each with 8 sides, so the bases have a total of $8\times2=16$ edges. Then there are edges connecting the two bases. With one edge for each vertex of a base, we have 8 edges connecting the bases. So the total number of edges is $16+8=\boxed{24}$.

Sure! An octagonal prism is a three-dimensional shape with two octagons as bases and eight rectangular faces connecting the bases. 

To determine the number of edges in an octagonal prism, we need to find how many edges each rectangular face has, and add them up. 

Each rectangular face has two pairs of parallel edges, which means it has four edges. Since there are eight rectangular faces in an octagonal prism, total number of edges in an octagonal prism:

4 edges per rectangular face x 8 rectangular faces = 32 edges

Therefore, an octagonal prism has 32 edges.

We will need to know how much sand Sean has, so we need to find the volumes of each of the two cylindrical containers. &12\text{ old containers}\cdot \frac{16h\pi\text{ cm}^3}{1\text{ old container}} \cdot \frac{1 \text{ new container}}{64h\pi\text{ cm}^3} \\

Sure, I can help you out with that! 

The volume of a cylinder is given by the formula V = πr^2h, where r is the radius and h is the height. 

Since the height of the old and new containers are the same, we only need to compare the radii to find the ratio of the volumes of the two cylinders. 

The ratio of the volumes of the two cylinders is (8/4)^2 = 4^2 = 16. 

So, to find how many new containers Sean will need, we can divide the total volume of sand in the old containers by the volume of one of the new containers. 

The volume of each of the old containers is π(4)^2h = 16πh. 

So the total volume of sand in the 12 old containers is 12(16πh) = 192πh.

The volume of one of the new containers is π(8)^2h = 64πh. 

Dividing the total volume of the old containers by the volume of one of the new containers, we get:

192πh / 64πh = 3 new containers

So Sean will need 3 new containers to transfer all his sand. I hope this helps!

Original File: datasets/MATH/MATH/test/geometry/757.json
Extracting Answer from: 
We know that $AG:AD = 2:3$.  Triangles $AMG$ and $ABD$ are similar, so $AM:AB = AG:AD = 2:3$.  Likewise, $AN:AC = AG:AD = 2:3$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Therefore, the area of triangle $AMN$ is $810 \cdot (2/3)^2 = \boxed{360}$.

Sure, let's solve your math problem!

Let $X$ be the point on $BC$ such that $GX$ is parallel to $MN$. Since $GX$ is the median of triangle $AGM$ parallel to side $AM$, we have $[AGM]=[AGX]$ and similarly, $[CGN]=[CGX]$. Also, $GX = \frac23 GM$, so $[ABC]=[AGM]+[BGN]+[CGN] = [AGX]+[BGN]+[CGX]$. Therefore, 
\[[AMN]=[AGM]-[AGN]-[GMN]=\frac12[AGX]-\frac12[BGN]-\frac12[CGX].\] 
But $[AGX]=\frac13[ABC]$ and $[BGN]=\frac12[BGC]=\frac14[ABC]$ and similarly, $[CGX]=\frac14[ABC]$. Thus, we have 
\[[AMN]=\frac12\cdot\frac13[ABC]-\frac12\cdot\frac14[ABC]-\frac12\cdot\frac14[ABC]
=\frac{3}{16}\cdot[ABC] = \frac{3}{16}\cdot810 = \boxed{151.875}.\] Therefore, the area of triangle $AMN$ is $151.875$.

This triangle's vertices are the intersection points of each pair of lines. The intersection point of $y=0$ and $y=x+4$ is (-4,0). The intersection point of $y=0$ and $x+3y=12$ is (12,0). To find the intersection of the last 2 lines, we substitute the first equation for $y$ and then solve for $x$. Doing so, we get \begin{align*}
x+3y&=12 \\
x+3(x+4)&=12 \\
x+3x+12 &= 12 \\
4x &=0 \\
x &=0
\end{align*} Thus, $y=4$, and the intersection point is (0,4). Let the base of the triangle be the side of the triangle on the $x$-axis. Because this side is between the points (-4,0) and (12,0), its length is $12-(-4)=12+4=16$. The height must be perpendicular to this side and through the final vertex. This is along the $y$-axis. Thus, the height of the triangle is just the $y$-coordinate of the other point, which is 4. Thus, the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 16 \cdot 4=8 \cdot 4=\boxed{32} \text{sq units}$.

Sure! First, let's sketch the triangle and see what we're working with. 

To do that, let's rewrite the equation $x + 3y = 12$ in slope-intercept form: 

$$ 3y = -x + 12 $$

$$ y = -\frac13 x + 4 $$

So, the three lines that bound the triangle are: 

- $y = 0$: the x-axis 
- $y = x + 4$: a line with slope 1 that intersects the y-axis at (0, 4) 
- $y = -\frac13x + 4$: a line with slope $-\frac13$ that intersects the y-axis at (0, 4) 

Now, let's find the vertices of the triangle (the points where two of these lines intersect): 

- For $y = 0$ and $y = x + 4$, we have: 

$x + 4 = 0 \implies x = -4$ 

$y = (-4) + 4 = 0$ 

So the first vertex is (-4, 0). 

- For $y = 0$ and $y = -\frac13x + 4$, we have: 

$-\frac13x + 4 = 0 \implies x = 12$ 

$y = (-\frac13)(12) + 4 = 0$ 

So the second vertex is (12, 0). 

- Finally, for $y = x + 4$ and $y = -\frac13x + 4$, we have: 

$x + 4 = -\frac13x + 4 \implies x = -3$ 

$y = (-3) + 4 = 1$ 

So the third vertex is (-3, 1). 

We now have the three vertices of the triangle: (-4, 0), (12, 0), and (-3, 1). 

To find the area of the triangle, we can use the formula: 

$$ A = \frac12 bh $$ 

where $b$ is the base of the triangle (the length of the line segment that lies along the x-axis) and $h$ is the height of the triangle (the length of the line segment that goes from the base to the third vertex). 

Since the x-axis is the base of the triangle, the length of $b$ is the distance between the x-coordinates of the two endpoints: 

$$ b = 12 - (-4) = 16 $$ 

To find the length of $h$, we need to find the distance between the line $y = -\frac13x + 4$ and the point (-3, 1). One way to do that is to use the formula for the distance between a point and a line: 

$$ \mathrm{distance} = \frac{ | ax + by + c | }{ \sqrt{ a^2 + b^2 } } $$ 

Here, $a$, $b$, and $c$ are the coefficients of the equation $y = -\frac13x + 4$, so $a = -\frac13$, $b = 1$, and $c = -4$. 

Plugging in the values, we get: 

$$ \mathrm{distance} = \frac{ | (-\frac13)(-3) + 1(1) - 4 | }{ \sqrt{ (-\frac13)^2 + 1^2 } } = \frac{ 10/3 }{ \sqrt{10}/3 } = \sqrt{10} $$ 

So the height of the triangle is $\sqrt{10}$. 

Finally, plugging in $b$ and $h$ into the area formula, we get: 

$$ A = \frac12 bh = \frac12 (16)(\sqrt{10}) = 8\sqrt{10} \approx 25.3 \text{ square units} $$ 

Therefore, the area of the triangle bounded by $y=0$, $y=x+4$, and $x+3y=12$ is approximately 25.3 square units.

Extracting Answer from: 
Let the perpendiculars from $C$ and $D$ to $BE$ intersect $BE$ at $X$ and $Y$, respectively.  These perpendiculars split trapezoid $BCDE$ into two isosceles right triangles $\triangle BCX$ and $\triangle EDY$ and one rectangle $CDYX$.

In isosceles right triangles (which have angles 45-45-90), the ratio of the leg length to the hypotenuse length is $1:\sqrt{2}$; hence, we have $BX=CX=DY=YE=12/\sqrt{2}=6\sqrt{2}$.  We also have $XY=CD=12$, as opposite sides of a rectangle are equal.

Thus, trapezoid $BCDE$ has bases of length $CD=12$ and $BE=6\sqrt{2}+12+6\sqrt{2}=12+12\sqrt{2}$, and height of length $6\sqrt{2}$.  Hence its area is $\frac{1}{2}(12 + 12 + 12\sqrt{2}) (6\sqrt{2}) = \boxed{72 + 72\sqrt{2}}$.
