To find the area of the trapezoid $BCDE$, we first need to find the length of the two bases. Because octagon $ABCDEFGH$ is a regular octagon, all of its sides are congruent to each other and so each is 12cm long. Draw diagonal $AC$ as shown below:

[asy] real x = 22.5; draw(dir(0+x)--dir(45+x)--dir(90+x)--dir(90+45+x)-- dir(90+2*45+x)--dir(90+3*45+x)-- dir(90+4*45+x)-- dir(90+5*45+x)--dir(90+6*45+x));

label("$A$", dir(90+45+x), W); label("$B$", dir(90+x), NW);label("$C$", dir(45+x), NE); label("$D$", dir(x), E);label("$E$", dir(90+5*45+x), E);label("$F$", dir(90+4*45+x), SE); label("$G$", dir(90+3*45+x), SW);label("$H$", dir(90+2*45+x), W);
draw( dir(90+x)--dir(90+5*45+x) );
draw( dir(90+x)--dir(45+x) );
draw( dir(90+45+x)--dir(90+3*45+x), dashed );
label("$12$", midp((dir(90+x))--(dir(45+x))),NW);
label("$12$", midp((dir(90+45+x))--(dir(45+x))),NE);
[/asy]

Triangle $ABC$ an isosceles triangle, so we can find the length of the diagonal $AC$ as follows: $$AC = 2\cdot AB\cdot \cos(45^\circ) = 24\cdot \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}=12\sqrt{2}.$$ Notice that the trapezoid $BCDE$ is made up of two right triangles: $\triangle ABE$ and $\triangle ACE$. We can use $\triangle ACE$ to find the length of the trapezoid as follows:

[asy] real x = 22.5; draw(dir(0+x)--dir(45+x)--dir(90+x)--dir(90+45+x)-- dir(90+2*45+x)--dir(90+3*45+x)-- dir(90+4*45+x)-- dir(90+5*45+x)--dir(90+6*45+x));

label("$A$", dir(90+45+x), W); label("$B$", dir(90+x), NW);label("$C$", dir(45+x), NE); label("$D$", dir(x), E);label("$E$", dir(90+5*45+x), E);label("$F$", dir(90+4*45+x), SE); label("$G$", dir(90+3*45+x), SW);label("$H$", dir(90+2*45+x), W);
draw( dir(90+x)--dir(90+5*45+x) );
draw( dir(90+x)--dir(45+x) );
draw( dir(90+45+x)--dir(90+3*45+x), dashed );
label("$12$", midp((dir(90+x))--(dir(45+x))),NW);
label("$12$", midp((dir(90+45+x))--(dir(45+x))),NE);
label("$12\sqrt{2}$", (dir(90+x)+dir(45+x))/2, SE);
[/asy]

Notice that $AC = AD + DC = 24$ and $\angle ACD = \frac{360^\circ}{8} = 45^\circ$. This means that $\triangle ACD$ is an isosceles right triangle, so $AD = DC = 12\sqrt{2}/2 = 6\sqrt{2}$. Then, we have \begin{align*}
CE = AC - AE &= 12\sqrt{2} - 12\\
AE &= AB + BE = 12+12=24.
\end{align*} By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle ACE$, we can find the length of $CE$: \begin{align*}
CE &= \sqrt{AC^2 - AE^2} = \sqrt{(12\sqrt{2})^2 - 24^2} = 12\sqrt{2}\sqrt{2-\sqrt{2}}.
\end{align*} Finally, we can use the formula for the area of the trapezoid $BCDE$: $$\text{Area} = \frac{1}{2}(BC+DE)(CE) = \frac{1}{2}(12+12\sqrt{2})(12\sqrt{2}\sqrt{2-\sqrt{2}}) = \boxed{72(3-2\sqrt{2})}.$$

Extracting Answer from: 
To solve the problem, we can use the Angle Bisector Theorem to find $BX$, and then use the Pythagorean Theorem to find $AX$. Let $m$ be the length of $CX$. Then, by the Angle Bisector Theorem,

$$\frac{BX}{AB}=\frac{CX}{AC}.$$

Substituting the given lengths, we get

$$\frac{BX}{30}=\frac{m}{21}.$$

Solving for $BX$, we find $BX=\frac{10m}{7}$. 

Now, consider right triangle $ABX$. We have

$$(AX)^2+(BX)^2=(AB)^2,$$

or

$$(AX)^2+\left(\frac{10m}{7}\right)^2=30^2.$$

Substituting for $BX$ and solving for $AX$,

\begin{align*}
(AX)^2+\left(\frac{10m}{7}\right)^2&=30^2\\
(AX)^2+\frac{100m^2}{49}&=900\\
(AX)^2&=900-\frac{100m^2}{49}\\
AX&=\sqrt{900-\frac{100m^2}{49}}.
\end{align*}

Therefore, to find $AX$, we need to know the length of $CX$.

First, we look at $\triangle MDN.$ We know that $DM = 4,$ $DN=2,$ and $\angle MDN = 60^\circ$ (because $\triangle EDF$ is equilateral).  Since $DM:DN=2:1$ and the contained angle is $60^\circ,$ $\triangle MDN$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Therefore, $MN$ is perpendicular to $DF,$ and $MN =\sqrt{3}DN = 2\sqrt{3}.$

Next, we calculate $CP.$ We know that $QC = 8$ and $\angle QCP = 60^\circ.$ Since $MN\perp DF,$ plane $MNPQ$ is perpendicular to plane $BCDF.$ Since $QP || MN$ (they lie in the same plane $MNPQ$ and in parallel planes $ACB$ and $DEF$), $QP \perp CB.$

Therefore, $\triangle QCP$ is right-angled at $P$ and contains a $60^\circ$ angle, so is also a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. It follows that $$CP = \frac{1}{2}(CQ)=\frac{1}{2}(8)=4$$and $QP = \sqrt{3} CP = 4\sqrt{3}.$

First, notice that the slice creates two congruent trapezoids $QPCD$ and $MNCD$ and a new pyramid $QPMN.$

Since trapezoids $QPCD$ and $MNCD$ are congruent, we can find the area $[QPCD]$ and then double it to get the total volume for the two. We have $MQ=16$, $CN=6$ (because $\triangle ABC$ is equilateral), and $ND=14$. Thus, the area of $QPCD$ is $$\frac{(QC+PD)(MD+NC)}{2}=\frac{(8+12)(4+6)}{2}=60,$$ and the total volume of the two trapezoidal prisms is $2\cdot(60)(12)=1440.$

For the pyramid $QPMN,$ we note that base $QNM$ is equilateral with side length $10,$ since $MQ=16,$ $MN=ND=14,$ so $QN=MQ-MN=16-14=2.$ Since the height of the pyramid is $12,$ its volume is $\frac{1}{3}\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}(10)^2\right)(12)=\frac{300\sqrt{3}}{4}.$

Thus, the total volume of the sliced region $QPCDMN$ is $1440+\frac{300\sqrt{3}}{4}=\boxed{1140+75\sqrt{3}}.$

For a circle of radius $r$ and an arc of $\theta$ degrees, the arc length is $(2\pi r)\frac{\theta}{360}$. Thus, for the same arc length, the arc angle is inversely proportional to the radius, so the ratio of the radius of circle $A$ to the radius of circle $B$ is $40:55$, or $8:11$. Since the ratio of the areas of two circles is the square of the ratio of their radii, the ratio of the area of circle $A$ to the area of circle $B$ is $(8/11)^2=\boxed{\frac{64}{121}}$.

Sure, we can solve this problem together!

Let's start by using the fact that the length of an arc in a circle is proportional to the measure of the central angle that subtends that arc. That means that if we let $r_a$ and $r_b$ be the radii of circles $A$ and $B$ respectively, and if we let $s_a$ and $s_b$ be the lengths of the arcs subtended by $55^\circ$ and $40^\circ$ respectively, then we have:

$$\frac{s_a}{s_b} = \frac{55}{40}$$

We can use the formula for the length of an arc, which is given by $s = r\theta$, where $r$ is the radius of the circle and $\theta$ is the measure of the central angle subtended by the arc. Substituting in our values, we get:

$$\frac{r_a\cdot 55^\circ}{r_b\cdot 40^\circ} = \frac{55}{40}$$

Simplifying, we get:

$$\frac{r_a}{r_b} = \frac{55}{40}\cdot \frac{40^\circ}{55^\circ} = \frac{4}{\pi}$$

(We can convert degrees to radians by multiplying by $\frac{\pi}{180}$.)

Finally, we can use the formula for the area of a circle, which is given by $A=\pi r^2$, to find the ratio of the areas:

$$\frac{A_a}{A_b} = \frac{\pi r_a^2}{\pi r_b^2} = \left(\frac{r_a}{r_b}\right)^2 = \left(\frac{4}{\pi}\right)^2 = \boxed{\frac{16}{\pi^2}}$$

So the ratio of the area of circle $A$ to the area of circle $B$ is $\frac{16}{\pi^2}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, here's the solution to the problem:

Let $AD = d$ and $BD = x.$ Using the angle bisector theorem, we have $\frac{BD}{DC} = \frac{AB}{AC} = \frac{3}{5},$ so $DC = \frac{8}{5} - x.$

By Stewart's Theorem on $\triangle ABC$ with cevian $AD,$ we have
$$
6^2\left(\frac{8}{5}-x\right) + 10^2x = d^2\left(\frac{8}{5}-x\right) + d^2x.
$$Simplifying and solving for $d^2,$ we get
$$
d^2 = \frac{6^2\cdot\frac{8}{5} + 10^2\cdot x - 6^2\cdot x}{\frac{8}{5} + x} = \frac{260}{8 + 5x}.
$$To find $x,$ we can use the Law of Cosines on $\triangle ABC:$
\begin{align*}
8^2 &= 6^2 + 10^2 - 2\cdot 6\cdot 10\cos C \\
\cos C &= \frac{11}{15}.
\end{align*}
Then, using the Law of Cosines on $\triangle ABD$ with the angle bisector $AD,$ we have
\begin{align*}
x^2 &= 6^2 + d^2 - 2\cdot 6\cdot d\cos \frac{A}{2} \\
&= d^2 - 72d\left(\frac{s-b}{bc}\right) \\
&= d^2 - \frac{72d}{80}\left(\frac{8-x}{x}\right) \\
&= d^2 - \frac{9}{5}d\left(\frac{8-x}{x}\right).
\end{align*}
Substituting $d^2 = \frac{260}{8+5x}$ and solving for $x,$ we get
$$
x^2 = \frac{36\cdot 260}{(8+5x)^2}.
$$
Now, substituting this value of $x^2$ back into the equation for $d^2,$ we obtain
$$
d^2 = \frac{260}{8 + 5x} = \frac{260}{8 + \frac{40}{3}\cdot\frac{6\sqrt{65}}{65}} = \frac{1040}{39}.
$$Therefore, the value of $AD^2$ is $\boxed{\frac{1040}{39}}.$

Triangles $AXC$ and $ABC$ share an altitude from $C$, so \[\frac{[AXC]}{[ABC]} = \frac{AX}{AB} =\frac47.\] Therefore, $[AXC] = \frac47[ABC]$.

Triangles $AXY$ and $AXC$ share an altitude from $X$, so \[\frac{[AXY]}{[AXC]}=\frac{AY}{AC} = \frac{6}{10} = \frac35.\] Therefore, $[AXY] = \frac35[AXC]$, so \[[AXY] = \frac35[AXC] = \frac35\cdot \frac47[ABC] = \frac{12}{35}[ABC],\] which means that the desired ratio is $\boxed{\frac{12}{35}}$.

Certainly! We will use some basic geometry concepts to solve this problem. 

First, let's draw a diagram. 

[Image of triangle ABC with points X and Y on sides AB and AC respectively]

By connecting points $X$ and $Y$ to point $C$, we have divided $\triangle ABC$ into three smaller triangles: $\triangle AXY$, $\triangle BXC$, and $\triangle CYB$. 

We know that the area of a triangle can be found using the formula:

$Area = \frac{1}{2} \cdot \text{base} \cdot \text{height}$

Let's start by finding the area of $\triangle ABC$. We'll call the height of the triangle $h$:

$\frac{1}{2} \cdot 7 \cdot h = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 10 \cdot h$

$7h = 10h$

$h = \frac{10}{7}$

So the area of $\triangle ABC$ is:

$\frac{1}{2} \cdot 7 \cdot \frac{10}{7} = 5$

Now let's find the area of $\triangle AXY$. 

We know the base is $AX$, which is 4. We need to find the height, which is the perpendicular distance from point $Y$ to line $AX$. To do this, we can use similar triangles. 

Triangle $AYX$ is similar to triangle $ABC$ because they share angle $A$. This means that the ratio of their corresponding side lengths is the same:

$\frac{AX}{AB} = \frac{AY}{AC}$

Substituting in the given values, we get:

$\frac{4}{7} = \frac{6}{10}$

Simplifying, we get:

$\frac{2}{7} = \frac{3}{5}$

This is not quite in the form of a ratio we can use to find the height of $\triangle AXY$, so we'll cross-multiply:

$5 \cdot 4 = 7 \cdot 3$

$20 = 21$

Oops! We made a mistake somewhere. We should have gotten a ratio of $\frac{10}{21}$ when we cross-multiplied. Let's go back and see where we went wrong. 

We made a mistake in simplifying:

$\frac{2}{7} = \frac{3}{5}$

should be:

$\frac{2}{7} = \frac{6}{21}$

Now we can cross-multiply:

$5 \cdot 4 = 7 \cdot 6$

$20 = 42$

Still incorrect! Let's double-check our work. We should have gotten a ratio of $\frac{4}{7}$ for $\frac{AX}{AB}$ and $\frac{6}{10}$ for $\frac{AY}{AC}$:

$\frac{4}{7} = \frac{8}{14} = \frac{12}{21}$

$\frac{6}{10} = \frac{3}{5} = \frac{12}{20}$

Our mistake was in cross-multiplying the wrong way. We should have multiplied 7 by 6, and 5 by 4:

$7 \cdot 6 = 5 \cdot 12$

$42 = 60$

Now we can find the height of $\triangle AXY$:

$\frac{1}{2} \cdot 4 \cdot \frac{12}{21} = \frac{8}{7}$

So the area of $\triangle AXY$ is:

$\frac{1}{2} \cdot 4 \cdot \frac{8}{7} = \frac{16}{7}$

Finally, we can find the ratio of the area of $\triangle AXY$ to the area of $\triangle ABC$:

$\frac{\frac{16}{7}}{5} = \boxed{\frac{16}{35}}$

Phew, that was a tough one! Don't worry, with practice, problems like these will become easier.

Let the radius of the large semicircle be $6x$.  The diameter of the smallest semicircle is $\frac{1}{1+2+3} = \frac16$ of the diameter of the largest semicircle, so the radius of the smallest semicircle is $x$.  Similarly, the radius of the next smallest semicircle is $2x$, and the radius of the next semicircle is $3x$.  The unshaded area then is the sum of the areas of the three smallest semicircles: \[\frac12(x)^2\pi + \frac12 (2x)^2 \pi + \frac12(3x)^2\pi = \frac12(x^2 + 4x^2 + 9x^2)\pi = (7x^2)\pi.\] The largest semicircle has area $\frac12(6x)^2\pi = 18x^2\pi$, so the shaded area is \[18x^2\pi - 7x^2 \pi = 11x^2\pi.\] Therefore, the desired ratio is \[\frac{11x^2\pi}{7x^2\pi} = \boxed{\frac{11}{7}}.\]

Let the radius of each semicircle be $r_{1},r_{2},r_{3},$ and $r_{4}$ respectively. Then since the circumference of each semicircle is congruent, $\pi r_{1} = 2\pi r_{2} = 3\pi r_{3} = 4\pi r_{4},$ which leads us to $r_{1} : r_{2} : r_{3} : r_{4} = 1:2:3:4.$ 

Let $O$ be the center of the circle with diameter $AD.$ Then, $\angle AOD=90^{\circ}.$ Note that the semi-circle with diameter $AB$ passes through $O.$ So, the semicircle with diameter $AD$ is simply the result of subtracting the semicircles with diameters $AB$ and $BC$ from the semicircle with diameter $AD.$ 

The ratio of the sum of the areas of the three smaller semicircles to the area of the larger semicircle equals $\left(\frac{r_{1}^2}{r_{4}^2}+\frac{r_{2}^2}{r_{4}^2}+\frac{r_{3}^2}{r_{4}^2}\right) = \frac{1^2}{4^2} + \frac{2^2}{4^2} + \frac{3^2}{4^2} = \frac{14}{16} = \frac{7}{8}.$ The ratio of the area of the two unshaded regions to the area of the larger semicircle is $\frac{r_{1}^2+r_{2}^2}{r_{4}^2}=\frac{1^2+2^2}{4^2} = \frac{5}{32},$ so the ratio of the shaded area to the unshaded area is $\boxed{\frac{27}{25}}.$

Sure! To solve this problem, we need to use the fact that the volume of a cylinder is given by the formula V = πr²h, where r is the radius of the base of the cylinder and h is its height.

Let's start by finding the volume of P.J.'s mug. Since the diameter is 3 inches, the radius is 1.5 inches. Therefore, the volume of P.J.'s mug is:

V_P.J. = π(1.5²)(6) = 42.4 cubic inches

Now let's find the volume of Schuyler's mug. Since the diameter is 4 inches, the radius is 2 inches. We don't know the height, so let's call it h. Therefore, the volume of Schuyler's mug is:

V_Schuyler's = π(2²)(h)

We want Schuyler's mug to hold the same amount of juice as P.J.'s mug, so we set the two volumes equal to each other and solve for h:

V_P.J. = V_Schuyler's
42.4 = π(2²)(h)
42.4 = 4πh
h = 10.6/π

Since we want our answer as a mixed number, we can convert this to:

h = 3 and 1/3 inches

Therefore, Schuyler's mug must be filled to a height of 3 and 1/3 inches to hold the same amount of grape juice as P.J.'s mug.

We know where points $A$, $B$, and $C$ are, and we know that the shape is a parallelogram, so we know that the differences in the x-values and y-values between $B$ and $A$ must be the same as the differences in x-values and y-values between $C$ and $D$.

The solution is $\boxed{(-5, -4)}$, which keeps the vertices in alphabetical order, as shown in the first figure. Two other parallelograms are shown in figures 2 and 3 (where we number them from left to right), but they are not solutions because the points aren't in the correct order. They would be parallelograms $ACBD$ and $ABDC$, respectively. [asy]

Sure, let's solve this problem together!

Firstly, we know that opposite sides of a parallelogram are parallel and equal in length. Let's use this property to find the coordinates of point D.

We know that AB and DC are parallel and have the same length. We can find the length of AB by using the distance formula:

AB = sqrt[(7 - (-3))^2 + (12 - 5)^2] = sqrt[10^2 + 7^2] = sqrt[149]

Since AB and DC have the same length, we can find the coordinates of point D by starting at point C and moving 149 units in the direction of vector AB. Let's find the components of vector AB:

AB = <7 - (-3), 12 - 5> = <10, 7>

To move 149 units in the direction of AB, we need to scale AB by a factor of 149/|AB|. Let's find |AB| first:

|AB| = sqrt[10^2 + 7^2] = sqrt[149]

Now we can scale AB:

D = C + (149/|AB|) * AB = <5, 3> + (149/sqrt[149]) * <10, 7> = <12.06, 10.16>

Therefore, the coordinates of point D are approximately (12.06, 10.16).

I hope this helps! Let me know if you have any questions.

Extracting Answer from: 
We can start by finding the reference angle for $240^\circ$. To do this, we subtract the nearest multiple of $360^\circ$ (in this case, $2 \cdot 360^\circ = 720^\circ$) that is greater than $240^\circ$:

\begin{align*}
240^\circ - 720^\circ &= -480^\circ
\end{align*}

Since $-480^\circ$ is negative and lies in the third quadrant, we know that the terminal side of the angle $-480^\circ$ intersects the unit circle at a point with $x$-coordinate $-\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$ and $y$-coordinate $-\frac{1}{2}$.

[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0));
draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2));
draw((0,0)--(-sqrt(3)/2,-1/2),red,Arrow(6));
draw((0,0)--(-sqrt(3)/2,1/2),dashed);
draw((-sqrt(3)/2,0)--(-sqrt(3)/2,-1/2),dashed);

label("$240^\circ$", (-sqrt(3)/2,-1/2), SE);
label("$\frac{\pi}{6}$", (-sqrt(3)/2,1/2), NW);
label("$\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}$", (1.2,0), NE);
label("$\frac{1}{2}$", (0,1.2), NE);
[/asy]

Therefore, we have:

\begin{align*}
\tan 240^\circ &= \frac{\sin 240^\circ}{\cos 240^\circ} \\
&= \frac{-\frac{1}{2}}{-\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}} \\
&= \frac{1}{\sqrt{3}} \\
&= \boxed{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{3}}.
\end{align*}

Let $r$ be the number of inches in the radius of the can.  The volume of the can is $\pi r^2(\text{height})=6\pi r^2$ cubic inches.  The lateral surface area is $2\pi r (\text{height})=12\pi r$ square inches.  Setting $6\pi r^2$ equal to $12\pi r$, we find  \begin{align*}
6\pi r^2-12\pi r&=0 \implies \\
6\pi r( r-2 )&=0 \implies \\
r=0 \quad &\text{or}\quad r=2.
\end{align*} Taking the positive solution $r=2$, we find that the radius of the can is $\boxed{2}$ inches.

Sure! Let's start by denoting the radius of the can as "r."

The volume of a cylinder is given by the formula V = πr²h, where h is the height of the cylinder. In this problem, we're told that the volume of the can is equal to the area of the label that covers the lateral surface of the can. The lateral surface area of a cylinder is given by the formula A = 2πrh, where h is the height of the cylinder and r is the radius.

So, we can set up an equation: 

πr²(6) = 2πrh 

Simplifying this equation, we can cancel out the π on both sides: 

r²(6) = 2rh 

Dividing both sides by 2h: 

r² = 3r 

Now we can solve for r: 

r² - 3r = 0 

Factoring out an r: 

r(r - 3) = 0 

So either r = 0 (which doesn't make sense, since the can has nonzero size) or r = 3. 

Therefore, the radius of the can is 3 inches.

Since $\sin (180^\circ - x) =\sin x$ and $\cos (180^\circ - x) = -\cos x$ for any angle, we have $$\tan(180^\circ - x) =
\frac{\sin(180^\circ - x)}{\cos(180^\circ - x)} = \frac{\sin x}{-\cos x} = -\tan x$$for any angle for which $\tan x$ is defined.  Therefore, $\tan\angle BDC = -\tan\angle BDA$.

From the Pythagorean Theorem, we have $AC = \sqrt{BC^2 - BA^2} = 24$.  Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, we have $AD = AC/2 =12$.  Therefore, we have $\tan \angle BDC = -\tan \angle BDA = -\frac{BA}{AD} = \boxed{-\frac{7}{12}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Certainly! Let's first draw a diagram of the triangle $ABC$ with $D$ being the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$:


[asy]
pair A,B,C,D;
A = (0,0);
B = (7,0);
C = (0,24);
D = (A+C)/2;
draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(B--D, dashed);
label("$A$",A,SW);
label("$B$",B,SE);
label("$C$",C,N);
label("$D$",D,E);
label("$7$",(B+A)/2,S);
label("$25$",(B+C)/2,W);
[/asy]

We can see that $\triangle ABC$ is a right triangle with $\angle BAC = 90^\circ$, so we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find $AC$:

$$AC = \sqrt{AB^2 + BC^2} = \sqrt{7^2 + 25^2} = \sqrt{674}.$$

Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, we know that $AD = DC = \frac{AC}{2} = \frac{\sqrt{674}}{2}$.

Now let's look at $\triangle BDC$.  We want to find $\tan \angle BDC$.  We know that $\angle BDC$ is opposite the side $\overline{BC}$, so we can use the tangent function:

$$\tan \angle BDC = \frac{\text{opposite}}{\text{adjacent}} = \frac{BD}{DC}.$$

So, the problem is now reduced to finding $BD$.  To do this, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem again, this time on $\triangle ABD$:

$$BD^2 = AB^2 + AD^2 = 7^2 + \left(\frac{\sqrt{674}}{2}\right)^2 = \frac{845}{4}.$$

Taking the square root of both sides, we have:

$$BD = \frac{\sqrt{845}}{2}.$$

Now we can find:

$$\tan \angle BDC = \frac{BD}{DC} = \frac{\sqrt{845}/2}{\sqrt{674}/2} = \frac{\sqrt{845}}{\sqrt{674}}.$$ 

And that's our answer!

The volume of the $2$-cm cube is $2^3=8$ cubic centimeters. The volume of the larger cube is $8+19=27$, so the length of one edge is $\sqrt[3]{27}=\boxed{3}$ cm.

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's solve the problem!

The volume of a 2-cm cube is given by:

V = l x w x h = 2 cm x 2 cm x 2 cm = 8 cubic cm

The new volume is the volume of the original cube plus 19 cubic cm:

V_new = 8 cubic cm + 19 cubic cm = 27 cubic cm

Let x be the length of one edge of the new cube. Since the cube is formed by equal sides, the volume of the new cube in terms of x is:

V_new = x^3

Therefore, we can solve for x:

x^3 = 27 cubic cm

Taking the cube root on both sides:

x = 3 cm

So the length of one edge of the new cube is 3 cm.

I hope that helps! If you have any more questions, feel free to ask.

There are many ways to draw the diagram; one possibility is shown below.  We know that $\angle BAC \cong \angle CAD\cong \angle BCA$ since $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD$ and $\overline{AD} || \overline{BC}$.  Therefore $\triangle BAC$ is isosceles.  In the diagram we have added segment $\overline{BE}$ splitting $\triangle BAC$ into two smaller congruent right triangles.  We also know that $\triangle ACD$ is a right triangle by the givens, so we conclude that $\triangle ACD \sim \triangle CEB$ since we already know that $\angle CAD\cong\angle ECB$.  In fact, $\triangle ACD $ is exactly four times the size of $\triangle CEB$ since $AC=2(EC)$.  If we let $[\triangle CEB]=K$, then $[\triangle AEB]=K$ while $[\triangle ACD]=4K$.  Thus $6K=42$, so $K=7$ and $[\triangle ACD]=4K=\boxed{28}$.

[asy]
import olympiad; import graph; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); dotfactor=4;
int randangle = 50;
draw((-5,0)--(5*dir(randangle))--(5,0)--cycle);
path x1 = (5*Cos(randangle),5*Sin(randangle))--(-10,5*Sin(randangle));
path x2 = (-5,0)--(5dir(2*randangle));
pair X = intersectionpoint(x1,x2);
draw((-5,0)--X--(5*dir(randangle)));
draw(rightanglemark((5,0),(5*dir(50)),(-5,0),s=14));
draw(anglemark((5,0),(-5,0),X,18));
draw(anglemark(X,5*dir(50),(-5,0),18));
label("$A$",(-5,0),W); label("$D$",(5,0),E); label("$C$",(5*dir(50)),E);
label("$B$",(X),N);

pair L = foot(X,(-5,0),5*dir(50));
draw(X--L);
draw(rightanglemark(X,L,(-5,0),14)); draw(rightanglemark(X,L,(5*dir(50)),14));
label("$E$",L,SE);
[/asy]

Extracting Answer from: 
Sure, let's work on this problem. Because $\overline{AD}|| \overline{BC}$, we have $$\frac{CD}{AD}=\frac{BC}{AB}.$$ Since $[ABCD]=42$ and $\overline{AD}|| \overline{BC}$, we know that $$\frac{AD+BC}{2}\cdot h=42,$$ where $h$ is the height of the trapezoid. Therefore, $$\frac{AD+BC}{2}=\frac{84}{h}.$$ By the angle bisector theorem, we know that $$\frac{AC}{CD}=\frac{AB}{BD}=\frac{AD}{BC},$$ so $$\frac{AC}{CD}=\frac{AD}{BC}.$$ Since $\overline{AC}\perp\overline{CD}$, we have $\triangle ACD \sim \triangle ABC$. This implies that $$\frac{AC}{CD}=\frac{h}{AD},$$ and $$\frac{AD}{BC}=\frac{h}{BC+h}.$$ We can now combine the equations we have derived to solve for $[\triangle ACD]$, which is given by $$[\triangle ACD]=\frac{1}{2}AC\cdot AD=\frac{h^2}{BC+h}.$$ Substituting the expressions we have derived, we obtain $$[\triangle ACD]=\frac{h^2}{BC+h}=\frac{h\cdot AD}{2\cdot BC}=\frac{h}{2}\cdot \frac{AD}{\frac{BC}{h}}=\frac{h}{2}\cdot \frac{AD}{\frac{AD}{CD}}=\frac{h\cdot CD}{2},$$ or $\boxed{21}$.

We know that $OA$ and $OB$ are each radii of the semi-circle with center $O$.  Thus, $OA=OB=OC+CB=32+36=68$.  Therefore, $AC=AO+OC=68+32=100$.

The semi-circle with center $K$ has radius $AK=\frac{1}{2}(AC)=\frac{1}{2}(100)=50$.  Thus, this semi-circle has an area equal to $\frac{1}{2}\pi(AK)^2=\frac{1}{2}\pi(50)^2=\boxed{1250\pi}$.

Sure, first let me explain what I'm doing.

In this problem, we are given a diagram with three semi-circles that share a diameter (the line segment $CB$). The goal is to find the area of the semi-circle with center $K$.

To approach this problem, we need to use some knowledge of geometry and algebra. Specifically, we will be looking at the relationships between the radii of the semi-circles and the lengths of the line segments that connect the centers of the semi-circles.

Now, for the solution:

Let's start by labeling some of the important parts of the diagram. We already know that $OC = 32$ and $CB = 36$, so let's label $OB$ as $2r$ (since it is the diameter of the larger semi-circle) and $OK$ as $r$:

[asy]
pair A, K, O, C, M, B, X, Y, Z;
O=(0,0);
C=(32,0);
M=(50,0);
B=(68,0);
A=(-68,0);
K=(A+C)/2;
X=(0,68);
Y=(-18,50);
Z=(50,18);
path nom, bigc, middlec, smallc;
nom=A--B--(100,100)--(-100,100)--cycle;
bigc=A..X..B--cycle;
middlec=A..Y..C--cycle;
smallc=C..Z..B--cycle;
fill(bigc, gray(.5));
fill(middlec, white);
fill(smallc, white);
draw(smallc);
draw(middlec);
draw(bigc);
draw(A--B);
label("$A$", A, S);
label("$K$", K, S);
label("$O$", O, S);
label("$M$", M, S);
label("$C$", C, S);
label("$B$", B, S);
label("$r$", (K+O)/2, N);
label("$2r$", (B+O)/2, N);
dot(K);
dot(O);
dot(M);
[/asy]

Since $O$ is the center of the largest semi-circle, we know that $CB$ is the diameter of that semi-circle, which means $OB$ is $2r$.

Next, we can use the fact that $K$ is the midpoint of $OC$ to find $OK$. We can use the midpoint formula to find the $x$-coordinate of $K$:
$$x_K = \frac12(x_O + x_C) = \frac12(0 + 32) = 16.$$
And since $K$ lies on the $x$-axis, we know that $y_K = 0$. Therefore, $OK = r = 16$.

Now we have enough information to find the area of the semicircle with center $K$. The area of a circle is given by $\pi r^2$, so the area of a semicircle is $\frac12 \pi r^2$. Plugging in $r = 16$, we find that the area of the semicircle with center $K$ is:

Let's call angle $A$ and angle $C$ in triangle $ABC$ "base angles," since they are opposite sides of equal length. 

Since angles $BAC$ and $BCA$ are both 35 degrees, the base angles in triangle $ABC$ are each equal to $\frac{180 - 35 - 35}{2} = 55$ degrees.

Now, let's draw a line from $D$ to $BC$ (the line containing side $BC$), and call the point where it hits $BC$ point $E$. 

$\angle AED$ and $\angle CED$ are linear pairs, so they add up to $180$ degrees. Since $\angle AEC$ is also $180$ degrees (because it's a straight line), we have:

\begin{align*}
\angle CED + \angle AEC &= 180 \\
\angle CED + (55 + 55) &= 180 \\
\angle CED &= 180 - 110 \\
\angle CED &= 70 \\
\end{align*}

Finally, since $\angle CDA$ and $\angle CED$ are vertical angles (opposite each other), we know that $\angle CDA = 70$ degrees.
